YOUNG AUSTRALIA: “Well, boys, what yer fisliin fur?"
FISHERMEN: “Labour fish.”
YOUNG AUSTRALIA : “Wot bait are yer usein’ ?"
FISHERMEN: "Oh, same old bait—marmalade.”
YOUNG AUSTRALIA : “ Well, it’s my opinion yer won’t catch nothin’,"

SATURDAY, JULY 4, 1891.
The Fall of Maoriland.
The census-returns of Maoriland, as com-
pared to those of Australia, are exceedingly
solemn reading. The people of Maoriland
are about the only nation on earth which has
sobered up after a wild burst of borrowing
and financial drunkenness, and gone through
the awful process of recovery, and the spec-
tacle is an interesting one for several
reasons. It presents the same attractions as
a cemetery does to the aged and the con-
sumptive—-there is a certain dreary pleasure
in looking at the spot to which we are all
going ; and the place to which New Zealand
has gone is, beyond any possible shadow of
doubt, the place which Australia is going to.
The only difference between the two cases
is that the Australian people are more
reluctant than their neighbours to face the
inevitable, and therefore, when they go, they
will go more violently and will fall more
heavily. The ten years that have elapsed since
the census of 1881 have in Maoriland been
years of commercial darkness, unrelieved by
a single one of those riotous circumstances
which are miscalled “signs of progress.”
There has been no increase of population
worth mentioning—the cities, in fact, have
absolutely receded, and though the country
districts have advanced, the total increase
for the whole country is only 40,000 —a
miserable average of 4000 per annum. There
has been no vestige of a land-boom, and
property is worth, in many cases, not very
much more than half what it was a decade
ago. The mining revival fell very flat; the
average mining revival consists of 1 per
cent, genuine mineral and 99 per cent,
stock-exchange swindle, and though the
discoveries of a few years ago fur-
nished the mineral, there was no boom, for
the money for the stock-exchange swindle
was wanting. The Government has built no
new public works—it has even abandoned
some that it inherited in a half-finished con-
dition, and the famous Otago Central Rail-
way is turned into a promenade for cows and
other animals. There have been no new
banks established, for the sufficient reason
that those already in existence have been in
serious danger of foundering; the “Loan and
Mortgage Companies,” and “ Loan and
Mercantile Associations,” and similar insti-
tutions, have led a precarious and unsatis-
factory existence ; and the big Land Com-
panies are more or less on their beam-ends.
There has been no influx of foreign capital,
and no influx of foreign population, and
many thousands of the immigrants which the
Government paid for in the old days, have
cleared out again, leaving nothing but dead
loss behind them. About half the Civil
Service has been discharged, and the Govern-
ment official who remains is harder-worked
than a dock-labourer, and has had his salary
cut down to correspond with his increased
exertions. The Parliamentary honorarium
has been shorn, and the number of legip-
lators reduced in order to avoid the payment
of unnecessary members, and the Governor’s
salary has Been curtailed, and all the old
glory and glitter and wild prosperity of the
country has faded away, leaving nothing but
hard reality in its stead.
* * *
What the people of Australia have as ydt
failed to realise, however, is that this hard
reality is only the normal condition of things,
and that all the garish splendour 'that went
before it was as bogus as a soap-bubble glit-
tering in the sun. 1 lie reality is a little
harder by reason of the boom, and the i'nflffx
of foreign capital, and the other commercial
diseases which preceded it, but that is all.
The slow increase of population is a cause of
general lamentation, but still the population
•is keeping pace with the average increase of
the world at large, and no ’country can
reasonably ask more than that. There is no
reason why the population of the
Australasian States should ’expand with
frenzied rapidity, and it has only done so in
the past because of that feverish state of un-
natural prosperity brought about b.y the
borrowing of foreign money. The fact that
the cities of New Zealand have ceased to
progress, and that the movement is entirely
in the country districts, may seem a painful
circumstance to a nation that expends all its
energies on growing‘one or two huge, bloated
capitals, but as soon as the influx of foreign
cash comes to an end Australia must go
through precisely the same experience, and
the influx of foreign cash must come to an
end sooner or later, unless the whole earth
is coming to this continent for investment.
When Australia is reduced to living upon its
own resources Melbourne and Sydney will
probably commence a process of shrinkage
that will reduce them to not much more than
half their present dimensions. These two
great cities started their wild career of un-
natural obesity when the “influx of foreign
capital ” set in ; while it lasted they
increased fully five times as rapidly
as the country districts ; and without
it they have hardly any visible means of
support whatever. Neither their manufac-
tures nor their commerce are even half suf-
ficient to account for their existence. The
cities, in a land which exists mainly by
agricultural and pastoral pursuits, live by
preying on the country, aud under normal
conditions no pastoral or agricultural country
ever maintains much more than one-fifth of
its population in great urban communities.
In other words, when one-fifth of the popu-
lation lives by interest and commissions and
percentages, the burden is as great as the
country can endure without disaster. Conse-
quently, judged by the experience of other
lands, somewhat less than half the population
of Sydney and Melbourne lives by reason-
ably legitimate means ; the other half exists
mainly on the unnatural increase in the
value of city lands—caused by the influx of
borrowed capital, on booms, mining rackets,
miscellaneous finance, and similar ephemera]
swindles—on lending to each other money
that doesn’t belong to them, and in selling
to each other that kind of non-existent
wealth which is created in a night by print-
ing scrip, and which fades away again in the
morning ; and on buying from each other
gold that neither of them ever saw, and pay-
ing for it with money that neither of them
ever had. But when the influx of foreign
capital ceases half these nondescript trades
and professions will vanish away. Then the
country must come down to grim fact, and
live on cold realities, and when the people of
Maoriland were brought face to face with
this state of things about a hundred ship-
loads of financiers and commission agents,
clerks, money-lenders, auctioneers, land-
brokers, share - brokers, money - brokers,
patent agents, and similar hangers-on quitted
the country in disgust, and left the cities
empty. This state of things was erroneously
described as commercial depression, but the
term was hopelessly inaccurate. The real
explanation was that in the day of the
country’s financial insanity every man who
worked had to support another man who
loafed in an office, and the period of alleged
depression was the period when the nation
recovered its intelligence and got rid of its
loafers.
# # #
The ruinous falling off in the Maoriland
banking industry has also been cited as an
evidence of depression, when in reality it
was merely a proof that the country was at
last becoming sober, and when Australia
sobers up in the same way there will be the
same smash among the banking institutions
of this country. Maoriland, in the days
of its joyous intoxication, ran three great
local banks with scores of branches, and
helped to maintain half-a-dozen exotic estab-
lishments which also spread out like an
octopus throughout the country. Also it
supported about a dozen huge financial com-
panies, and a host of building societies, most
of which did a certain amount of banking
business, The population of the country
was less than 600,000, and there were banks
enough for 6,000,000 ; in fact, if all the in-
habitants of the province had deposited all
the money and discounted all the good paper
they possessed twice every day, there was
not sufficient legitimate business in the
country to keep these institutions going.
But they lived by the old, old device of bor-
rowing foreign capital, and lending it out
to the misguided native inhabitant upon
mortgage, and so long as the foreign capital
came in land-values went up, and while they
went up interest also ascended. The miser-
able agriculturist whose land was reasonably
Hvorth £lOOO, saw it rise to £2OOO under the
influence of this financial fever ; and then he
borrowed £lOOO at 10 per cent., and in the
course of 10 joyous years he had paid it all
away in interest. And about that time the
falling-in of the Public Works Policy broke
up the great rush of foreign'capital in one
act, and the land went back to its true esti-
mate,' and was barely worth the mortgage-
money which had been borrowed and repaid
and yet remained to be repaid 6ver again.
Then both borrowers ; and lenders came to
grief, for the former were left Ipenniless ;
while the latter foreclosed and kept on fore-
'Closing until they found that they had bought
up half Maoriland with foreign capital.
And as they had to pay interest oh the
foreign capital, while their leagues of bank-
rupt property paid them nothing, the great
banking industry became eminently pre-
carious, if not actually ruinous. It ivas the
only possible result that cc/uld ensue, and it
is exactly the same result which must ensue
in Australia when the financial drunk of the
last 25 years comes to an end. A portentous
smash among the Australian banks and
financial institutions, a huge falling off in the
.population of the great Australian cities, and
a tremendous fall in land-values, will be the
first sure signs that this country is at last
beginning to live on its own industry, instead
of on its lOU, as heretofore.
* * *
Maoriland, within the last ten years,
has sent ont a good many more emigrants
than it has received, and—despite the public
lamentation which would inevitably ensue—
it would be well for Australia if it had clone
likewise. The stoppage of immigration
means that the Maori islands are no longer
an attractive field for General Booth’s
'“reformed criminals,” for the surplus thou-
sands Who now over-crowd the cities of Eng-
land, and who want to over-crowd the cities
of Australasia instead, for the cast-off
“remittance-man,” and the übiquitous
“ fool of the family.” As for the exported
inhabitants, Maoriland has lost very little
of the bone and sinew of its population ; it
counts some 50,000 more hardy miners and
agriculturists than it did ten years ago, and
it has fired out many thousands of city men
—of lawyers, clerks, financiers, brokers,
stone - brokers, merchants, shopkeepers,
bookkeepers, hotel-keepers, money-lenders,
betting-men, and others who live on the
community, yet add practically nothing to
the national wealth. Most of these have
drifted to Australia, which now—by virtue
of the bogus prosperity which is born of
foreign capital supports the aggregate
loafers of Grey’s country in addition to its
own. And when, through the blessing of
Providence, Australia comes to grief in like
manner, it will pass these acquisitions on to
some foreign shore, and send along with
them 100,000 or more of land-boomers, pro-
perty-agents, barristers, solicitors, convey-
ancers, building-society directors, bankers,
patent-medicine liars, book-agents, impor-
ters, civil service dudes, superfluous railway
employes, pawnbrokers, preachers, bagmen,
totalisator and racing-sweep fakirs, pedlars,
fan-tan experts, and others ; and by thus
wrecking its boasted census returns it will
create a better and a stronger nation out of
what is left.
* # #
In a country which, unlike England, has
no great income from foreign investments,
the annual exports and imports are a fair
criterion of the national progress. Despite
all the elaborate theories of Freetrade
economists the one unanswerable argument
remains—the exports of a country are the
means by which it pays for its imports, and
therefore when the imports are in excess the.
country necessarily runs in debt to the ex-
tent of the balance. At present the exports
of Australia require to be some £14,000,000
per annum ahead of the imports in order to
pay the interest on public and private debts
and yet keep the balance even ; in reality
they are some £10,000,000 behind, which
means that every year Australia owes
£24,000,000 more than it did the year
before. In the days of its alleged prosperity
Maoriland occupied a very similar situa-
tion ; in the present day of alleged
adversity it is reducing its external liabili-
ties by some £3,000,000 per annum. It is
enabled to do this mainly by the fact that
its country population—which produces
the national wealth—is expanding, while
the city population—which produces little
and consumes much—is slowly undergoing a
decline. And Australia must get square by
the same process—or else repudiate; the
choice lies between the wholesale con-
fiscation of foreign capital, or a hideous
■ shrinkage in the inflated, overgrown, much-
boasted cities of this continent. Two such
places as Sydney and Melbourne cannot be
maintained, on cash principles, by the scanty
population of two empty provinces, and
because these communities are proportion-
ately much larger than Auckland, Christ-
church, or Dunedin, the inevitable falling
off will be all the greater. Possibly it will
be better for Australia that the ruin should
come. New South Wales and Yictoria are
both ruled by the useless non-producers of
the two great capitals. They are governed
by landlords, importers, lawyers,’financiers,
and bankers—in other words, they are
governed by the men who were artificially
created through foreign capital. And when
the foreign capital ceases to flow in, as it has
already done in Maoriland, and the useless
cities that were built by its agency begin to
find their true level, half the birds of prey
whose plural votes now rule the State will
light olifc for other shores, and a good pro
potticrii of the remaining half will take a£n
axe apiece, and go to the woods, arid beednie
of some use to humanity fdr the first tinie.
“Representative Government!” What
It Has Done.
ThAt wonderfully mediocre person, who crops'up
rather unexpectedly in One of the works of
Herbert Spencer as “ Albert the Respectable,”
once observed that “Representative Government
was on its trial.” This prolific and imich-puffed
German princelet, unlike Charles 11. of obscene
memory, “never did a wise thing,” and said
foolish things as the sand on the seashore fdr
multitude. The above is to be taken as one of
the latter, but the irdny of the unexpected and
the evolution of history have given the phrase A
meaning, utterly different to that which its utterer
intended, but most profoundly true.
© % *
The rule, and by rule we merin the despotic
rule, of the genuine British aristocrats dates fridtii
the expulsion of the Stuarts, and the e'ollapse of
the ridiculous “divine tight of kings ” theory,fn
the so-called “Great Revolution ”'of IXSBB. The
genuine British aristocrats Vvei'e the colossal land-
owners—the tarett who still survive in the shape
of the Dukes 'Of Westminster and the Dukes of
Devonshire.' These men used the popular revolt
Against the secular and religions tyranny of
Charles 11. and Jame s 11., or the Puritan
revival, for their own utteily selfish class interests.
There were to be no more Cromwells and Com-
monwealths,'thank you, with shopmen or yeomen
or artisans rising to become admirals like Blake,
or generals like Ireton, or Protectors’ private
secretaries like Milton. ‘No ; the country was to
be run by the colossal landlords and for the
colossal landlords, Whose yoUngbr sons were to
administer army, naval and civil service as the
organised soldiers and police of their grace's arid
their lordships. From lt>Bß 'to 183-2 —almost A
century and a-half—this despotic rule lasted.
'# % *
The results Were briefly these : : A corruption of
the legislature, which, under its supreme manipu-
lator, Lord Walpole, reached a'pitch unparalleled
AaVe in the rottenest days of Roman and Spanish
aristocrat corruption, the days of Verres and tile
monsters of the Inquisition. Almost all the
new great private . fortunes date from this
period, and Smith, the contractor, whose descen-
dant is the downy Lord Cakington of to-day,
may stand as a familiar type of the novi homines,
or brand-neW nobles who arose from the plunder
of the people. So much for the interior adminis-
tration. Externally we have to note the wars arid
their result in that monstrous so-called “ National
Debt,” whose millions of interest drag annually
at the throat of the contemporary British tax-
payer. The National Debt, a device whereby the
dominant class could wage war purely in its oWtt
interests, and lay the whole burden of the cost
thereof on the nations, dates from the “gbeat
Revolution.” Of these wars the one Which
wa3. the most prodigious and shameless Write
the relentless war waged against the French
Republic and its oofttineater, the Empire, the Wat
which broke the French naval poVver at Trafalgar
and its military power at Waterloo, arid introdiiced
England and the eoritineht to the iniquities of the
Holy Alliance arid the partition ‘of Boland arid
Italy,
* * *
The Ererich Republic was the protagonist of the
Rights of Matt. It preached “Liberty, Equality
and Fraternity.” It is still customary for the
English to speak of Napoleon as an ambitious
tyrant, and Waterloo as the glory of a
free people that overcame a people of
slaves. This, now, in the impartial light
of History, grows thin even to emaciation !
The Code Napoleon, which is still the) law of
France and which France has clung to pas-
sionately through Restorations and constitu-
tional Monarchies and bastard Empires and
bastard Republics—which the German Rhine
provinces have clung to equally passionately, and
which rules even to-day in German Alsace and
Lorraine instead of the German law ! —this Code,
which we hope to see Australia adopt almost
in its entirety during our own lifetime,
is the most stupendous legislative effort after
equality the world has yet seem It makes ah
equal division of land and wealth amonga man’s
children compulsory. It strangles entail with one
hand and disinheritance with another, and makes
huge estates all but an impossibility. Similarly
the civil and military administration of Napoleon
was an equally stupendous effort after liberty and
fraternity, and (to use the Emperor’s splendid
words) “ La carriere ouverte aux talens ” —tools for
the hand that can use them! He took farmers
and workmen, as Cromwell had done, and made
them colonels or generals, magistrates or
ministers, princes or kings. Is it any wonder
thatthearistocraticcountries —England, Germany,
Austria, Russia—where birth alone made possible
rule, military, naval or civil, would not—could
not —rest tili this man had conquered them or
they him ?
* # •
The vast bulk of the English “National ” Debt,
then, is the history of the frantic and at last
successful effort of the British aristocrats to over-
come Napoleon, and the ideas he incarnated, and
strangle republican France into humility. This
debt was not all merely expenditure on the army
and the navy. It came also from the gigantic
subsidies paid by England to its miserable allies,
the kingly cut-throats of Berlin, Vienna and St.
Petersburg. Well, at last they succeeded.
Napoleon was sent to be worried to death in St.
Helena, and France trembled under a throne
supported by foreign bayonets, and Europe was
“ at peace.”
# # #
So much for the rule of the genuine British
aristocrats. By 1815, however, the date of
Watei’loo, the brand-new aristocrats had added
solidity to the body and force to the ener-
vated souls of their dissolute predecessors and
partners. But more! The potent middle-class,
the shop-keepers, had come upon the scene. Their
power, though real, was wholly unrepresented in
the legislature no less than in the services.
Napoleon called England a nation of shop-
keepers, because he saw that it was from
the shop-keeping class that the floods of wealth
which paid her soldiers and sailors and subsidized
those of her allies proceeded. 1 le did not see that
the middle-class was then merely the tool of the
aristocracy. And for long the middle-class did
not see it. Then began its awakening, and with
it the horn of the final agitation against “ rotten
boroughs.” “Rotten boroughs” were boroughs
returning the nominees of the great nobles.
Originally they had been under the control of the
Crown, that is, from their foundation under
Edward VI. (1547) right up to the expulsion of
the Stuarts and the fall of the power of the kings.
The middle-class had the numbers on their side,
and they had plenty of money. The result of the


bombat could not, then, long be doubtful. In
1832, accordingly, after having used thb Masses
as their cats’-paw in all the more dangerous parts
Of the Work, the aristocrats gave way ; the Reform
Bill was passed, the Masses swindled by a pro-
perty qualification which deprived them of the
power to vote, and the middle-class entered on its
co-rule with the aristocracy. Thus the Houses of
Lords and Commons, as we at present know them
were formed, and when the late lamented Albert
the Respectable made that foolish remark with
Which we opened, he meant that'this new arrange-
ment was then “on its trial.
#' # #
Over half-a-cehtury has passed since this neW
was initiated, and its results are now
hatfeht to all; The trial has ended either this
way o’r that, so’ far,- either in failure or in success.
Let its co’nsldet this, the rule of the middle-class,
m the, sairie simple,’ historical Manner as we have
considered the former iule, the rule t>f the aris-
tocrats. < ,
* * *
In 1815; then; followed what is called “The
(Treat Peace.” The Continental peoples, the!
peoples of France, Spain, Italy, Germany, and
Austria, were resolutely held down and almost
the whole energy of men’ was cast into the new
forms of industry. Machinery made its appear-
ance, and the creation’ of' wealth received an
impetus unparalleled' in all history. More sheer
Wealth has been created ih the last half ceritury
than in any other five, or even ten centuries.
This is the distinctive feature of our age—though
theoretic and applied science, the discoveries of
the great Scientists, and the application of these
discoveries by the great mechanicians'. The result’
tias beeil remarkable. In the old Feudal days
itte colossal land-owners supported Vast armies of
fetainefi? Who' gaVri td them their services as
Soldiers in return fOr shelter, food, and’ general
protection. Machinery, by enabling the rich to
offer \yhat appeared’ to be fcjfo means of independ-
ence to the m'asses', seemed to harmonise at once
the modern demands fof iridivithial freedom and
for the nevv impetus of the creation 1 Of wealth.
Capital and Labour became the “two utterly dis-
tinct entities” of Adam Smith and the race of
“ political economists ” which culminated in John
Stuart Mill and his crowd of attendant satellites,
# * *
O potent and damnable fallacy! This is the
distinction between Capital and Labour. Among
the old feudal retainers the principle of compe-
tition was dormant. They had certain work to
do and duties to perform, and in return they were
secure of decent maintenance. Not*so among the
modern Masses ! Competition being unrestricted,
diorfe arid ttlore did the wretched men sacrifice the
iVorth 6f their work to the ever-increasing
Exigencies 8i existence resultant in improvements
of machinery arid thfe condensation of Labour.
Capital scooped in! thfe riett total of the sacrifice,
and the New Slavery Was aSn established fact.
Were the old feudal 1 lords tyrants 1 Perhaps so,
but at lpa'st they housed arid fed therf; slaves,
whereas the new capitalists washed their hrinds of
qyerything but the payment of these “wages,”
which left these “Britons who iiriVep (never,'
never !) would be slaves ” to’ toil arid starve and
rot to death as they pleased ! And all the while,
the Press rang with the paeans of “unexampled
progress,” “liberty and the same laws for all,”
and (final acme and apotheosis of a “Christian
civilisation !”) “ Representative Government /”
* * *
Thus that gigantic and terrible process, a pro-
cess which even the new Staffords have blessed
as meeting the ideal policy of “ thorough,” began
—the process of the degradation of Labour ! The
very commercial slang terms bore witness to it.
People spoke no longer of workmen in the fac-
tories : they were only “hands.” What, verily?
Had you drilled the hearts and souls out of them
so well as that? Yea, verily, and, what is more,
the heads also. For the masses were the numbers,
and these millions of sheep who sought the capi-
talistic shears with such a stupid stoicism, held
also the remedy. True, they made some efforts
against their fate. Bands of starving, desperate
men went about the country breaking machines
when the capitalistic trick was first sprung upon
them. But aristocratic lord and middle-class
commoner ancient noble, brand-new noble,
gentleman, shopman, priest—all were utterly at
one In putting down a “ lawlessness ” which
threatened not only the present case but the
stupendous future prosperity which was just
drawing oh their intelligences, That prosperity
was only possible by means of machinery, and,
therefore, for Machinery they (backed up by their
Soldiers, sailors, and police) Would perish ! There
Was (happily) no need for that, They had only to
deal With a ttiob of mad dogs, and the “special
Constables ” Of 1848 are the notable predecessors of
the great Exhibition of 1852, when one of their
dumber, Louis Napoleon Bonaparte, the
debauched scoundrel Who had Meantime “ waded
through blood to a throrie,” inaugurated in France,
as our precious Queen and her mutton-headed
Consort did in England, the neW Holy Alliance
Of Birth and Capital against strangled and
degraded Labour.
# * *
Labour’s next efforts seemed at last in si better
direction, in the direction of organisation and drill.
These efforts we know by the name of Trades
Unionism. Trades Unionism meant the first
attempt at co-operation of the thousand oppressed
small units against the one oppressing big
unit. But how hopelessly restricted was its pro-
gramme from the very start, and how doomed to
ultimate failure ! Trades Unionism never faced
the great initial iniquity of the plunder of Labour
bv competition wages. Trades Unionism never
held up the banner of the cause of Labour one
and indivisible. All Trades Unionism then tried
to do was to keep up wages where they were, and
to save the skilled workmen. It tinkered the
old, worn-out kettle, and by letting a part of
Labour, large in itself but comparatively quite
small, enjoy a mild competency, robbed Labour
as a whole of its best men—formed an aristocratic
class in its own ranks to trample on the
mass below it—put off the (lay of a final
reckoning for a whole generation, and
made the victory of the middle-class utter
and complete. It did more and worse! As
the tide of democracy—and that is the tide of
Labour—slowly rose, this better class took the
brib9 of an extended franchise to support the
aristocrat and the middle-class in retaining the
abominable status quo, They took this bribe from
Disraeli in the Reform Bill of 1807, and they
gave him his Imperialistic and Jingo Dictatorship
of six years, from 1874 to 1880,
# # *
This is the history of “Representative Govern-
ment,” the history of the degradation of Labour !
A.nd what a success it has been, what a success
for the robbers, great and small, whose ease and
wealth has sprung from it ! The toiling and
starving millions—the London labourer and the
country agricultural labourer reduced to the level
of brute beasts—man, wife, and ohildun to be
; upported on a wage which averages under 10s.
a week—a great swarming mass of stupid and
debauched humanity, these are the foundations
On Which is reared “ the stately edifice of- British'
liberty,” “the Unexampled progress,” “ the
justibe and mercy of a Christian civilisation of
Representative Government /” Representative
Government ? Representative of what ? Take
the roll of its legislators issued by the PALI Mali,
Gazette, and run it through. Out of the 600 and
odd men who make up its House of Commons, will
you l find 40 who have not been l educated at the
English public-schools—at Eton, Harrow”, Rugby,
Marlborough, and the like? Will you-find five
genuine working-men? You will not! Gentle-
men and rich shopkeepers, these and* none but
these; lawyers, officers, and men of leisure of
the aristocratic schools, aiid the tribe of capitalists
and- employers, these are 99 per cent, of this
‘ Representative Government.” But what Aus-
trahan needs to be assured of this when* he can see
with his own eyes' the class of men* who have
hitherto represented bmf Squatters, law-
yefs, doctors, employers, commission-agent's, these
are out Australian equivalents, and what are they
but a* lower-grade replica of their English fellows?-
# #* *
This is the fiistoty, the pitiful history,- of the
degradation of Labour in England and the rule
of the middle-class and “Representative Govern-
ment.” It is the same in Americait* was
rapidly becoming the same in Australia.- “On
trial,” O 5 prolific, much-puffed and mfitfon-
beaded German Princelet ? Nay, on itsr trial no
longerapparent as the rtiost hateful of all

bas ground men slowly and surely to powder,
qq ie apathy of the sacrificed masses is passing,
Labour arouses itself to make Democracy, the
power of tlie People, felt and feared. Despair is
a rude but thorough educator. The hour of final
reckoning comes closer. It comes closer in
Europe. It comes closer in America*. It comes
closer in Australia. “So completely,” says Henry
George—HenryGeorge, the man of “law and
order” and “ constitutional methods so com-

"Betterment.”
The Victorian Railway Commission lias dug out
a curious little circumstance which is of interest
not only to the people of Victoria, but to those
of all Australia. It has ascertained, in an acci-
dental sort of way, that the money that has
been borrowed to build the Victorian railways
has all been borrowed unnecessarily—that aU
the load of indebtedness is a fraud and a swin-
dle—and that a good deal more than £30,000,000
has befeii fooled away in one little province
through Wefe idiocy and embezzlement and
general " Alid as the facts on which
tliis conclusion is based apply equally to New
South Walds,- Queensland, and the other States
of Australasia, the natural inference is that
£150,000,000, or thereabouts, lials beet# scattered
quite unnecessarily, and that the entire load of
indebtedness is a dead loss from beginning to'
end. The basis of calculation is very simple.
While investigating one of the many expensive
and unprofitable railways in the vicinity of
Melbourne, it was ascertained that this line—
which was paid for by the nation —had boomed
the adjacent property to such an extent that
the entire cost of the work might have been
charged, on the betterment principle, to one
big landholder alone, and still that worthy
citizen would have been £400,000 to the good.
From this point some further investigations led
to the conclusion that all the justifiable railways
of the province might have been built by levy-
ing assessments on the proprietors of land in
the vicinity, and the increase in values caused
directly by these railways would have not only
covered the assessments, but would have left these
same proprietors much richer than they were
before j and no doubt the situation is much the
same in other parts of the continent. In other
words, the country should have got its railways
for nothing ; there should have been no debt at
all; the income derived from the lines should
have been so much clear profit to the revenue ;
and the landholders who paid for these lines
would still have been richer than if they had
not been constructed and paid for. Instead of
this, however, the railway system has been a
dead loss twice over. When £1,000,000 was
spent by the country on building a new line,
the adjacent landlords straightway started
selling their lands to the unhappy taxpayers
at £2,000,000 advance—in other words, the
people paid £1,000,000 for the railway, and
because they had done this, they had to pay
£2,000,000 more for the privilege of living
where they could have the use of it. Then the
landlord, being enriched by the £1,000,000
spent by the nation on improving his property,
and by the £2,000,000 which the nation had to
pay afterwards for the privilege of giving away
the £1,000,000, most probably invested the
loot and then started for “ Home and now
the country owns a railway which is a dead
loss, and pays interest on £3,000,000 for the
jov and gratification of possessing this loss.
The Victorian Commission is now trying
to formulate some scheme whereby there
will be no more borrowing for railway
purposes, and all new lines will be charged to
the adjacent landholders, and even this arrange-
ment-should it be carried into effect—will
leave the Victorian land-shark in a remarkably
good position. The thirty millions or so which
the people have given him in the past is
obviously irrecoverable, and the other thirty
millions which he has made by adding insult to
injury is also gone beyond repair. And if, in
the future, he be required to pay the entire cost
of railway construction, he can do so and still
make 50 or 100 per cent, profit on the outlay.
Under the Land Nationalisation principle, not
only would the land pay for the railways and
leave the country without any debt whatever,
but the additional value gained by these im-
provements would extinguish all other taxation,
and place the State in affluence. In other
words, instead of Australasia having a railway-
debt of some £150,000,000 it should own the
railways and be something over £150,000,000 to
the good in addition. The dicsrepancy between
these figures is just three hundred millions, and
the landlords of Australia, Tasmania, and Maori-
land have scooped the lot.
Latter-Day Bigotry.
Let us revert to a> subject the serious cUscussioni
of which is,, wa admit,.am insult to human intolr
ligence. The other day a youth, who was lilting,
in a sentence of three years at Pen triage,
tired of the weary panorama of white-washed;
walls and iron bars, and resolved to cut the
matter short by suicide. His life had been a
failure all through, however, and his hanging
was ore a par with the-rest of it,,for it failed
also. He was cut down by a> benevolent official,,
and when the visiting justices came round they
“ considered.” his case,*and passed a> sentence or
12 months’ additional imprisonment,, and M
days’ “solitary” for “insubordination.
Possibly the clam-headed individuals who passed
this sentence believed that if a? prisoner was
tired of life 12 months’ imprisonment ancl.l4 days
“ solitary ” was just the thing to* cheer hinv<up
and make him desire to live —that it was just
the thing which would deter from-, suicide
any other prisoner whose brain had given way
and who longed for death—there is nothing
on this earth that is absolutely impossible with
a 1 visiting magistrate. The eminent gaol* official
in the Australian novel, “ Moondyne,” had a
female prisoner flogged within an inch of her
life for not praying, and when she found that
she’ had either to pray or to keep* on being
flogged-,» she started praying right off'. Still
there are some objections to such a process. In
the present eas®' the prisoner gets 12 months
because he is not of the same religious opinions
as the magistrates who* sentenced biniy. and the
magistrate who uses his authority to* jump on
another man on account of his religion is the
meanest kind of a persecutor, and ought to have
had a-billet to heat the*pincers and oil the rack
in the' Inquisition. The magistrate himself
didn’t commit suicide, either because lie; was too-
comfortable in’ this world to want to take any
unnecessary risks in ! another, or else because
his religion stood' in the way-—and in order to
give him the benefit of the doubt, and-to*make
him look a shade less contemptible, we will
assume the latter to have been the reason.
The prisoner (if sane—it would lie horrible
brutality to send to “solitary "’ an insane
prisoner) attempted suicide, on the other hand,
because his religion did not stand in the way.
There is absolutely not one objection to suicide
except the objection that is based on religion,
and, therefore, the difference between the two
men was a purely religious difference. If the
magistrate had been a Wesleyan, and bad
ordered the prisoner to be flogged for being a
Presbyterian, bis conduct would not have been
one shade less mean or stupid or narrow-minded
or irrational. The theory that the offender’ got
his additional sentence for “insubordination
is mere hypocrisy, because his insubordination
consisted entirely of the one circumstance that
his religious views, or his want of religious
view’s, allowed him to attempt self-destruction
—in other words, he broke tlie gaol regulations
by* not believing in a blazing Slieol to come, and
if the regulations demanded that he should
believe in Slieol then they are an unspeakable
disgrace, and the men who enforce them are
another. Tlie one rational view of the ease is
this —Every human being has a right to hang or
drown or poison himself if be wants to. The
law* allows him to kill himself slowly with
drink—so long as he doesn’t become a nuisance
in the process —or tobacco, or by over-eating,
or by painting the city red on winter nights
and catching cold as a consequence; and it
allows him to lose his soul—if he has a soul—
and to go to Gehenna—if there is any Gehenna
—by being an atheist, or an agnostic, or a prize
bigot, or a hypocrite. And if the State has
any possible business to arrest a man for trying
to lose his body and his soul—providing he has
a soul, as above-mentioned—at the end of a
rope, then it is the duty of the State to flog
the atheist and imprison the bigot for risking
their souls, and to imprison influenza and
prospective bronchitis. And as the law has just
sense enough not to do these things it thereby
acknowledges that every man has a right to
die when he feels so inclined. The magistrate
who sends any man to gaol for attempted
suicide is a persecutor of the old inquisitorial
type, and, except that he has only half the
courage of his convictions, the difference
between him and Torquemada is not worth
swearing at.

Bed-headed Women and the Franchise.
Here is a conundrum for that alleged states-
man, Premier Munro. Munro declares him-
self to be a believer in the extension of suffrage
to all women, but because it is needful, in such
cases, to insert “ the thin end of the wedge,” he
suggests making a beginning by granting the
suffrage to women with property, and after-
wards extending the system by degrees. Now*,
allowing for the sake of peace, and out
of respect for old - established drivel, that
there is any w*edge at all, w’ould it not be an
equally sane proceeding to break the shock to
the public by extending tlie suffrage to red-
headed women first, and then let in other
shades of females by slow instalments ? Unless
Munro is a bigger ass than he looks, and this
seems hardly probable, he must be aware that
a woman with money doesn’t necessarily know’
more than a woman without money; and
therefore the only merit of his plan is that it
lets the public down gently, by not shoving
all the women in at once. He would get exactly
the same result by extending the franchise to
red-headed ladies, and as there are females with
flame-coloured locks in all stations of life he
would thereby give all classes an equal show.
Or, again, lie might tone things down by ex-
tending the suffrage to girls with warts as a first
start, or to unmarried women aged 50, or to
women w*howere dead broke, or to widow’s over
(or under) a certain age. All these schemes would
equally insert the thin end of that much-
drivelled-about wedge, and there is as much
sense in confining the suffrage to women with
warts as to women with dirt —which is earth,
otherwise called Property. But it is one of the
great evidences of the old-established abase-
ment of humanity that very few seem to see the
screaming absurdity of giving anybody a \ote
because that person has dirt. Even the
opponents of the principle only oppose it—they
hardly ever laugh at it, and yet the idea is so
hideously absurd that if Munro really means it
seriously he oqgliti to be shoved; into a lunatic
asylum.. In,fact, the man,who. would propose
such a thing ought to be- madi enough tp be
ahov,edi into, six, lunatip asylums.

New South Wales, but, in view of recent
events,, the Press mouthpieces of the Pas'
toralists! Association are gingerly beginning to
urge their patrons to back down. Bombastes
Fgrioso, who, three months ago, was prepared,
to shoot down anybody who “ dared, these-
boots displace/’ is now trying.to. ‘‘roar like-a.
sucking dove.”' Thesquattocraiao-press actually
advise—yes,, advise- —squatters to accord] a con-
ference to the shearers. These dangerous and
violent people don’t deserve any consideration,
at all, but— noblesse oblige,, and the generous
squatter is besought to condescends Of courses-
this temerity on. the part of the squattocratic
press is tempered with the salve of. a little bom,-
bastic praise, and the contumacious shearer is-
adhdsed that—
If such a thing should happen again these- ean he no-
question.that the shearers will suffer far more than the
employers- far more than they or any other o£ our wage-
earning. Glasses have yet suffered through similar causes,
heavily as that has been.— Sydsby D.T.
That threat cannot scare off destiny.. The-
workers may,, and very probably will,, suffer,
worst at the first, but their goal is a glorious
victory at last. The threat had been preceded
by a very misleading statement as to the result
of the late maritime strike :
It has failed to bring any advantage whatever to th,
strikers, while inflicting injury upon them alt and,
plunging many, in distress from which they have not yet
recovered..
It has brought this- advantage to- the workers,,
and to shearers as, workers among others—that
Labour has been effectually aroused to a full;
sense of its wrongs a>nd its power* In the words
of the D.T., Labour unionism—
Has come to be regarded as-a dangerous and tyrannical,
force.
Employers’ unionism, of course-, is a healthy
and desirable force. The admission is signifi-
cant. It has been wrung from capitalists by
the triumphs at the polls of the Labour-party
in New South Wales. The voice of the ballot-
box is the answer of the people to- the insolent
arrogance that says—
They (the squatters) insist upon drawing their future
supply of labour from wherever it may be obtainable.
And until their right in this respect is fully conceded by
the unions they decline to enter into Conference with,
them.
Let them “ insist.” They can’t Gatling-gun a
Legislature in which Labour determines the
balance of power, and they arc not such fools
as not to know it. The rest is skite. There
will be a Conference right enough.

Bursting the Big Estates.
The Australian democracy is coming uppermost,
and the old, stereotyped politicians are bidding
for the support of the masses whom they have
hitherto held in such small esteem. Parkes’
old, mildewed, moth-eaten Local Government
Bill, and his newly-borrowed “ One Man One
Vote” scheme, and his shapeless, undefinable
project for improving the relations between
Capital and Labour, are all baits for the
suffrages of the Labour party. Munro’s de-
clared intention of taking that hazy thing
commonly defined as “steps” to minimise the
influence of wealth in the Legislature is a means
to the same end. And now the lumbering and
ponderous Playford, of South Australia, w hose
clumsiness has been the subject of so many
jibes, has gone one better than either of these,
and struck a brighter inspiration than Pakkek
and Munro combined. His war-cry is the
bursting up of the big estates —the same politi-
cal whoop which did Graham Berry so much
service long ago—and if the reality is anything
like the promise Playford intends to go the
whole of that animal which the average big
estate-owner so much resembles. He proposes
that no large landed property shall he handed
down by inheritance—it must he cut up and
sold in small allotments; and as a provision
against the formation of new estates out of the
fragments of the old ones he undertakes to im-
pose a tax on all newly-formed properties of
over 500 acres, which will blast the proprietor
financially, and make him wish that he had
never been born. This proviso will meet the
case of the individual landholder, and in order
to deal with the great land conspirators,
banks, mortgage companies and the like, it is
intended to give to tenants the right to pur-
chase their holdings at a valuation either with or
without the concurrence of the bloated pro-
prietors of the soil. Further, in case the
tenants should he unable to exercise this right
to any great extent, the Government intends to
resume the overgrown properties which are
now being held for a rise by all manner of joint-
stock absentees, and fix the compensation by
arbitration, and trade them off again—
Parliament will probably grant long leases
and not sell at all—in small allotments,
on easy terms. This scheme is not Social-
ism, neither is it Land Nationalisation,
but* still it is very decent Liberalism. It
recognises the principle that the landlord can-
not °do what he likes with the land simply
because lie has bought it, because land is
essential to existence, and the rights of man are
of more account than a Torrens’ title. It is
also an unfee 1 ing attack on the great axiom of
Monopoly that unwritten axiom according to
which any man entitled to kill oft a whole
community by making a “corner in food or
water or air, and holding these substances for
a rise, always provided he has acquired them in
due legal form. And, though Playford is as
far from being a reformer as Parkks or Munro,
it is a favourable sign that the nation is shoving
him into new paths The Democratic force
that can push a man of his size in front of it
is a thing of might, and is strong "'Vmgh to
shunt mountains and elephants and tons of lead
off their bases, and’to butt old vested interests
into the ?ea, and to move even the oldest and
heaviest of Conservatives into a better frame of
mind

W. i DOUGLAS. Manairer.
The first Queensland loan—the one which was
floated in 1866—has just been paid off by the
simple process of borrowing some more money
to pay it with, and the country has now a
chance to figure out the profits on the trans-
action. In 1866 Queensland sold securities to
the amount of £1,170,950 on the London
market, which brought in actual cash about
£l,ooo,ooo—in other words, a trifle over one-
tenth of the money vanished in discount and
“accrued interest,” or, on a rough estimate,
about £120,000. The interest was 6 per cent.,
at which rate Queensland paid some £1,576,500
tor the use of the money it actually got, and
about £IBO,OfKI for the alleged use of the money
it didn’t get. Then, wlTen the loan expired, it
became necessary to raise a new loan to pay off
the old one, and this new loan is also at 10 per
cent, discount—in other words, Queensland
borrows about £1,300,000 to pay off the original
sum of £1,000,000, and after another spell it
will probably become necessary to borrow
£1,500,000 to pay off the £1,300,000, and so on
ad infinitum. Meanwhile, the situation stands
thus : Queensland raised a little over £1,000,000
in actual cash, and it has already paid £2,000,000
in interest and commissions for the use of it,
and it still owes the original amount as much
as ever ! This isn’t bad for a 25 years’ record.
\\ hat a truly blessed thing is the influx of foreign
capital !
The Ballarat “mutiny, 5 which promised to
result in several hundred able-bodied men being
rescued from that state of mental darkness of
which an uniform is the outward visible sign,
has not turned out a blessing for the country,
after all. Fora time Major-General Tri.LOCH
and his mob of gaudy incubi swore great oaths
under their cocked liats to the effect that the
whole Ballarat militia must be disbanded—-yes,
disbanded, bai Jove ! —and made a shocking
example of. Two hundred of the men had
refused to “ fall in,” or something, so Tcllock
would have to go down to the scene of this
awful disgrace, and say—“ Men of the 3rd
Victorian Regiment, you are no longer soldiers
of the Queen, nothing is left for you
lint suicide, or a lingering death from re-
morse.” Then it slowly dawned upon the dull
regimental minds of these snorting “authori-
ties ’ that nobody wished a lot of militia not to
be disbanded, or would care if there were no
army left. Likewise, Tullooh and Co. began to
reflect that the colonels and things w r ho clatter
round in charge of the disaffected troops would
be suddenly thrown upon the bleeding country’s
hands, as it were, if the troops retired into
private life. So it was decided to give the 3rd
Regiment one more chance to wipe out the
aforementioned disgrace. Major-General Tul-
loch ordered a solemn ‘ ‘ compulsory ” parade
(from which 134 men stayed away) and then
told each man in turn that he w r as a noble fellow
who musn’t be disbanded on any account,
because he was so phenomenally loyal and broad
across the chest. After which another profes-
sional blatherskite asked all the men who had
mutinied to step forward and save this magnifi-
cent regiment from disbandment—he knowing
perfectly well that 134 malcontents hadn’t
turned up to step forward. Whereupon 51 of
those present stood out and grinned, which so
affected Tullooh that he wept, saying these 51
were nobler Romans than the others, but every-
body was as noble as possible, and, thank God !
they wouldn’t be disbanded. Then the 3rd
Regiment laughed in its sleeve and the Heavens
groaned aloud.

Governor Kintore has drawn up a report
about his hurried rush across the continent,
and presented the document to the S.A. Govern-
ment. “He considers that in the present circum-
stances it would be ruinous for South Australia
to construct a trans-continental railway, but
recommends that the work be given to a land-
grant syndicate. ” Now, in the first place, it is
no part of Kintoke’s business to consider any-
thing ; he is paid principally for his entire
want of consideration. Secondly, he has
nothing whatever to do with the circum-
stances; they aren’t his circumstances, and he
isn’t fit to judge a circumstance, anyhow.
Thirdly, the land-grant syndicate racket is the
vilest swindle alive, and it would cost the
country ten times more in the end than it would
to make the trans-continental railway at the
cost of the Treasury. It is a device by
which a syndicate gets several million acres of
land for making its own railway, and it
also gets the land required for railway pur-
poses gratis, and it imports all its material and
gets it in free of duty by special concession, and
it brings in cheap Italians or Chinese or coolies
to do the work, and turns them loose when the
job is finished. Then the syndicate gets up a
scheme for selling its line to the Government,
and runs the project through Parliament by
wholesale bribery; and the Government accord-
ingly takes over a jerry-built line that was made
to sell, and pays more for it than it would have
paid if it had built the whole concern itself at
t.he outset, and the two or three million acres
of land are all dead loss and swindle. And
either Kintore is an ass and doesn’t know this,
or he is something else, and intends to take a
hand in the syndicate. Also, in any case, Kin-
tore is not authorised by the Constitution to
give advice to the Government about railways
or anything else. He is paid to keep quiet,
and if he doesn’t keep quiet he shouldn’t be
paid.

At Sydney Her Majesty’s, on the arrival
of “The Lyons Mail,” the manner in which
William Rignokl was received must have warmed
the heart of that worthy man and fine actor. The
play, we believe, is identical with that staged by
Henry Irving as “ The Courier of Lyons,” an
adaptation from the French. The piece is now
well staged; the set of the second scene of the
first act, an auberge at Lieursiant, by Messrs.
Clint and Owen, being remarkably well
painted and designed. As the double William
the Great shines most as Dubose, the rogues’
champion ; his Lesurques, otherwise played with
freedom and jollity, partially failing to depict the
despair that must have overcome the well-to-do,
good-hearted Lesurques as he felt the halter of
circumstantial evidence tightening round his
throat. But Dubose’s murder-scene, interviews
with the betrayed girl, and the great scene where
he is entrapped in his garret, went with a con-
vincing bang and a snap not usual in the melo-
drama of this city. For the others, Harry
Jewett is somewhat too stalwart to imper-
sonate the effeminate masher-thief, Courriol,
nevertheless, his performanee left little room for
fault-finding. Edith Blande’s Jeanette is about
the best thing she has given us. The horse-coping
scoundrel ofJack ” Sweeney followed somewhat
closely on the lines of the Dubose, but was other-
wise clever. Joe Tolano’s philosophical pick-
pocket so captured the gallery that when he failed
to take a call at the end of an act in which he had
little share, one of the gods immediately
inquired, in an injured, expostulatory tone:
“What’s the matter with Joe?” and the
deities would not be pacified till Joe was
trotted out. The other parts are well handled
by Bessie Rignold, Arthur Greenaway, Frank
Harcourt, Jim Hazlitt, Stirling Whyte (though
somewhat too implacable), Arthur Rigby, and
Fanny Reynolds, whose Joliquet deserves favour-
able mention. La Bernhardt opens at this house
on July 8 in “ Camille,” and her season will con-
sist of 19 nights and three matinees. Prices
dress-circle and reserved stalls, 10s. ; stalls, ss. ;
family-circle, 4s. ; gallery, 2s. Hardie and Gor-
man will sell all the previously-unsecured seats
for the premiere by auction on Thursday after-
noon at their rooms, and the box-plan will be
opened on the following day.

At Sydney Garrick the farcical comedy,
“Our Flat,” seems likely to fall flat enough. Its
authoress, Mrs. Musgrave, became possessed of
an original and humorous central idea—the seizure
of time-payment furniture by bailiffs and its
replacement by artistically-draped tubs, barrels,
cases and planks—but she lacked the skill and
experience to weave a story round this and to
make the most of her opportunities for getting fun
out of essentially humorous situations. There-
fore, the first act is slow as an election
with only one candidate; in the second
the fun is intermittent, and the curtain
drops at the wrong time, while in the third there
is nothing to be done except to revive the business
of the second act, and tumble over the shaky
chairs and into the bath-tubs, and to wind up
with the “Heaven bless you, my children,” con-
clusion, whose always apparent approach moves
an audience even quicker than the “National
Anthem.” Teddy Royce and Polly Emery are
the soul and body of the show ; they have the best
chances and make the most of them, though
why Manager Glover is not introduced earlier
is not apparent. They are rather ably and
brightly played up to by Mary Kingsley, who is
however, just a little too hard in style, while
Lawrence Cautley, as the young author, lacks the
ease and lightness necessary for work of this
character. Edwin Kelly, as the heavy father,
rants and roars too much ; the character should be
played “ straight ” and without burlesque. Marie
Brooke and Hans Phillips are bright and easy in
small parts. Charles Brown, W. F. Clitheroe,
and Mrs. Hill score the utmost possible in abbre-
viated opportunities. “ Our Flat ”is not likely to
run for two years in Sydney as it did in London ;
we give it two weeks at the Garrick.

Goethe says : “ Men show their character
in nothing more clearly than by what they think
laughable.” Which reminds us that “ Evange-
line ” has been doing tremendous business at
Sydney Criterion. The Gaiety crowd will also
attract the multitude when it arrives, and the
chief difference between the disconnected, pur-
poseless, highly-gilt tommyrot it will fetch along
and that vended by the present company is that
the one sort, rightly called burlesque, is up to
date, while the other, misnamed opera bouffe, is so
sanctified by age as to pan out good as new to the
later generation. However, it may be said in
favour of “ Evangeline ” that it is splendidly
staged and played for all it is worth. Eortescue’s
song, “The man in the moon,” very well sung,
has a familiar twang; and “I like it” is an old
music-hall friend. The dashing and well-
proportioned Eileen Karl’s Amazonian guard
furnishes, possibly, the best leg-show this city has
seen—certainly the best-attired limbs that have
ever walked into the affections of Sydney's
masherdom. Lena Salinger quits the show to
join Tilly, now a prime favourite, in San
Francisco. “ Evangeline ”is good for a long run.

At Sydney Royal the “ Gondoliers ” are
still pursuing the primrose path that leads to a
bloated treasury. Elsie Cameron—she has our
gondolances—has been laid up with cold, and
Maud M‘Kay has reigned in her stead. The
Opera Company open at Brisbane on July 11th,
with Henry Bracy at the prow of management,
and their place will be filled by the Rignold
Brothers, who will open a three weeks’ season in
Mark Melford’s new play, “Flyingfrom Justice.”
Mr. J. P. Macdonald, as previously announced,
will swap offices with the genial G. L. G. for this
occasion only.

The Achurch farewell benefit, in “Adrienne
Lecouvreur,” was a huge success, artistically and
otherwise, and it seems a pity that this play, in
which Mrs. Charrington puts in her best work,
was not utilised long prior to her departure. The
audience was played out, reluctantly, with “ Auld
Lang Syne.” The Charringtons strike London in
October.
At popular prices the Hallo’s are nearly
filling the Sydney Town Hall. Better supported
than on a prior visit, it is now possible to sit out
the programme without feeling that certain num-
bers must be endured for the sake of what follows.
Madlle. Marie Fillunger, not as we had supposed
a newly-caged songstress, is a most business-like
artiste, who puts on no frills, either of style or
costume, but who has a very sweet, though not
powerful, highly-trained and sympathetic voice.
Madame Marian Burton, who is very well, thank
you, and has assumed a more rounded appearance
since she assumed matrimonial cares, ii in very
fine voice, though she challenges unfavourable
comparison with Patey in her choice of songs.
Norman Neruda is beyond criticism, and Halle,
r.s of old, toys with the piano as does a mother
who soothes and caresses her little one. Mr. J.
E. Sykes acts as accompanist, and the fifth
concert of the series takes place this (Thursday)
evening.

The Ivanhoe Minstrels will give a per-
formance with full orchestra at Bondi Aquarium
this (Thursday) evening. A big show and novel-
ties announced for Saturday.
The Gaiety Company made their re-appearance
at Melbourne Princess’ on Saturday night, before
an audience quite as overflowing and almost as
enthusiastic as that which gave them their first
Austral welcome three years ago. During those
years the Cockney notion of sparkling fun appears
to have stood as still as a cab-horse sleeping on
the rank. “ Ruy Bias ” is a trifle more incoherent
and broken-up than “Monte Cristo.” but it is
conducted on exactly the same rules as regards
the distribution of “fat,” and contains many of
the same little jokes and ingenious bits of business.
Fred Leslie fits the rim of a hat over a specially-
constructed bald head, so as to seem to be wearing
a fawn-coloured billycock, and the public mirth,
when he suddenly removes the rim, knows no
bounds. Formerly, in the character of a monk,
he used to shift a fringe of hair with equally sue
cessful results. On the present occasion, too,
Leslie enlarges upon his original imitation of
Irving by introducing him as a short-skirted
ballet girl, in conjunction with Danby and two
other male performers, who are generally under-
stood to be impersonating Toole, and a couple of
actresses (names unknown) under the same elevat-
ing conditions. Leslie, likewise, pops up in a
double character, one side of him being Scotch
and the other Irish, which parti-coloured perfor-
mance will be strikingly novel to all who haven’t
seen Johnny Gourlay’s more elaborate treatment
of the same old idea. Thinking over the humour
of a Gaiety buries pie, one is struck by the fact
that it is, to a considerable extent, mechanical
and worked by a string—like the good old
English Punch and Judy. Leslie and Nellie
Farren are quick-change artists of the highest
possible type. Nellie has as much intelligence
and “go ” as her companion star, but the want of a
bald-head and numerous other broad-comedy
effects put her ata serious disadvantage. She looks
nice, as usual, however (like the historical Miss
Smith), and whatever her legs find to do they in-
variably do it with neatness and dispatch. Leslie
gets through an enormous quantity of work
with a skill which is none the less remark-
able because it might be devoted to a higher class
of entertainment, whilst Fred. Storey’s dancing
retains its pristine vigour. Danby is rather lost
in a poor part, and Sylvia Grey does nothing
phenomenal with her feet in tins show. Sylvia,
in short, seems but a shadow of her old self. She
acts not, neither does she spin as merrily as of
yore. Florence Levey is in no respect quite
equal to Letty Lind, whose place she takes, but in
a general way Florence is a nice girl, all the same.
So with Grace Pedley, the substitute for Marion
Hood, and ballad-songstress of the company.
Otherwise there is a liberal display of feminine
loveliness associated with the average amount of
Gaiety-girl intelligence. The stage presents a
very brilliant aspect when they are all “ on,” and
the scenery, by Goatcher and Brunton, is simply
superb. “ The Bogie Man ” will probably be re-
garded as the best of several good musical num-
bers. For this song and dance Fred. Leslie
assumes the irrelevant disguise of a sweep, the
“Whist, Whist!” chorus being supplied by a
background of girls in night-dresses. The tout
ensemble is weirdly fetching, although the air is
not so prettily turned as that heard at the Opera
House, a while ago, in “ Chilperic.”
Ms ms ms ms
“Run Wild,” produced at the Opera
House on Saturday, is hardly likely to run wild
for any great length of time. The name of the
party who wrote this three-act comedy doesn’t
appear on the programme, but he (or she) belongs
to the school of authors who classify their various
characters between brackets. Thus a certain
Monty Drury is set down as “an easygoing
chappie,” whilst a lesser luminary is hastily dis-
missed as “Another Chappie.” Lady Grace
Howard (“a good-natured girl”) calls upon the
chappie who is easy-going with a jewel-case in
her hand. Never having met him before in her
life, and not knowing his name, it naturally occurs
to Lady Grace to ask the easy-going one whether
he would mind taking her to the nearest pawn-
shop, where they can “ pop ” the jewels in the
interests of a third party. Seeing that they
immediately depart on this charitable errand, it
seems superfluous to explain on the programme that
Lady Grace is good-natured and the chappie easy-
going. Such conduct speaks for itself. Miss
Jennie Lee figures in large type as Collie Barker
(the Light of the Household), and her leading
speciality is a ferocious terrier, which, under
strong provocation, can be induced to make a
feeble pretence of wanting to bite a flunkey. The
part is no better suited to Jennie Lee than to any-
body else. It gives her no opportunities for
turning somersaults, or displaying any of her
often proved ability in the “ romp ” line of charac-
ter. Moreover, in any ease the little woman
couldn’t make a success of a dull, badly-written
thing like “Run Wild.” She might let them
have a little more “ Jo,” in which there is always
pleasure for the public and generally a profit for
the management.
M* Ms * *
At the Alexandra a steady demand for
‘The Trapper ” has set in. A furious bar-room
scene, and the poetic tableau, “After the Battle,”
seem better than ever, but in most respects “'The
Trapper ” is not the least bit better than it ought
to be, Considering that a tribe of Indians and a
tank ten feet deep in the middle are there when
wanted. A landslip, of which great things were
expected, turns out to be nothing worth shudder
ing at, more especially as the party who is slid
down into the tank by a convulsion of nature
looks uncommonly like a different man to Dr.
Carver.

“The Magistrate” caused “ House Full"
to be hung against the Bijou portals, on Saturday
night, and the chances of Pinero’s comedy running
for a second week looked as rosy as a parlourmaid
who has been wrestling with a “ young man ”
on the stairs. Brough, in the name-part, is sup-
ported by at least four of the originals, in Tither-
adge, Boucicault, Mrs. Brough (who met with a
great greeting), and Jenire Watt-Tanner. Cath-
cart makes a good sketch of the other beak, who
isn’t so “damned conscientious ” as poor old Posket,
and Cecil Ward gets wet through on the balcony
with much success.

The Royal opens again on July 25th with
Jones’new play, “The Dancing Cirl,” for which
Miss Laura Viiliers and a specially-selected com-
pany are just on the verge of arrival in these
parts. The Melbourne halls and other shows are
progressing as usual, and shall have a friendly line
next week.
There is not the slightest chance for tire
Melbourne people to see any more of Sara Bern-
hardt this journey. She plays her week in
Adelaide, then thi’ee weeks in Sydney, followed
by a week in Brisbane. From Brisbane she
returns to Sydney, and with a gracious smile
takes the boat for America. Sara will allow it to
be stated that she is unutterably grieved at. the
thought of leaving Australia so soon, but as a
matter of fact she will shed no tears at parting.
She, like every other true Frenchwoman, cares
for no country but France —no one who has lived
long in France cares much for any other country—
and takes no interest in any people but the French.
She has come out here under contract to perform
a certain number of times, and her chief business
care is to see that a certain guarantee sum for
each performance is handed to her every morning.
Over and beyond that Sara stands to benefit con-
siderably by big business, so that the element of
speculation enters into her engagement. Yet her
want of punctuality and long and frequent
“waits ” are not consistent with an earnest desire
to please her good friends the public or secure the
management against loss. Sara doesn’t like
altering her dilatory Parisian habits to
please foreigners, even though she might
have to forego her percentage of profits
as the penalty of waywardness. But, on the
other hand, she cannot bear to see the house
thinning during a performance, and the sight of
one person walking out whilst she—Mon Dieu !
holds the stage will move her more than all the
apologetic protests of Messrs. Abbey, Williamson
and Garner put together. “Let them go if they
don’t like it,” is said to be Sara’s scornful remark
when the gods are tired of gazing at the ever-
lasting curtain. But if they do go, it is the
divine one who doesn’t like it, which explains, in
a great measure, the reason why “Theodora,”
even more than “ Cleopatra,” was pulled together
after the first night. The indignation caused by
the “Theodora” prcmiire was fully justified by
results. On Thursday they played the tragedy
twice over, and the evening performance was
finished some 05 minutes earlier than on the Mon
day. Thursday happened to be the SnciiU Fran
cuis night, when the stage was loaded with flowers,
the audience rose up at the “ Marseillaise,” ind
Sara’s sympathies were all round the house.

The small amount of vice-regal patronage
bestowed upon Sara at Molbourno Princess’s
may possibly be accounted for by the fact that it
was better business to fill the Governor’s box with
ten-shilling seats than invite his gracious presence
on the nevah. There could be no sense in having
the Hopetouns and “ suite ” stuck in the centre of
the dress-circle unless his lordship were prepared
to pay for the space at current rates, like any other
person, which privilege the thrifty young Scotch-
man by no means hankers after. The Ilopetouns
went once to “ La Tosca ” in their V.R. capacity,
and once they saw “ Camille ” from the manager’s
box. This latter visit was not announced in the
advertisement column. Williamson and Garner
are possessed of some slight sense of humour, afld
although they love their Governor to distraction,
they presumably found that the notion of him
conferring his “distinguished patronage” upon
Madame Bernhardt was a bit too funny.

The speculative Abbey is already talking
airily of another Bernhardt visit at some not
vfery distant period. But he has yet to discover
how much the ill-effects of Sara’s “ waits ” may
be felt during the present trip even, without
taking into consideration the fact that a repetition
of the Melbourne boom would be impossible unless
the management could guarantee the regulation
of performances according to railway time-tables.
No such guarantee can be given, apparently. On
more than one occasion Madame Bernhardt has,
apparently out of pure indifference for the Mel-
bourne public, mingled with a spirit of sheer
cussedness towards the gentlemen who were tear-
ing their hair at the portals of her dressing-room,
compelled hundreds of her patrons reluctantly to
go away without seeing the last two acts of the
play they had paid for. The argument that no
time was fixed for the conclusion of, say, “ Theo-
dora,” and that the public must make allowance,
&c., is altogether a false one. A dragged-out
performanee involves a premature departure of
those who come from distant suburbs, or the ex-
penditure of a sovereign, more or less, in cab-hire.

In either case they consider themselves “got
at.” A ten-shilling ticket didn’t buy a complete
evening’s entertainment, unless the purchasers
were prepared to sink another fifteen shillings in
travelling expenses. “Theodora” and “Cleo-
patra ” contain fewer lines, and far less of heavy
scenery, than most of the melodramas which
Bland Holt manages to rattle through in three
hours. Two acts of “Theodora” occupy just 23
minutes between them, whilst the six intervals
are sometimes allowed to average 20 minutes each.
Nay, a brief twelve minutes’ act, mainly consist-
ing of spectacle, is sandwiched between two 24
minutes slices of curtain, that being at the rate of
twelve minutes of tragedy to the hour. The story
loses all continuity when thus spread about like
plums in a workhouse pudding, and it is hard to
understand how Sara Bernhardt, an artiste who
loves her work, can find any satisfaction in spoil-
ing its effect upon the audience. The Melbourne
people were very good to Sara, so long as they
believed that she was at least capable of that
gratitude for admiration which is one of the most
lovable characteristics of ordinary womanhood.
But after the first night of “Theodora ” the awful
slander that Sara doesn’t care a tinker’s curse for
folks in front became spread abroad. Then did
level-headed men sum up the situation in a calm,
judicial manner “ She’s a great actress. She
keeps us waiting three-quarters of an hour to
begin with, and another blessed half-hour after
every act. She’d cut each act in half and double
the number of intervals if she could, so long as
she got our money all right. Nobody pays us the
compliment of coming before the curtain to apolo-
gise for Sara’s playing the fool with us, and there
we sit, cramped and miserable, wondering whether
she means to go on with the show. She is a won-
derful actress. We shall never see another like
her and a jolly good job, too.” When Sara
reaches Sydney she will surely find the public
“ up ” to the tricks she played upon the spiritless
Melbournians, who never attempted to obtain
their legitimate rights. Let the Sydneyites insist
that each performance be advertised to conclude
at a reasonable hour, and demand their money
back if the contract isn’t duly carried out and let
J.C.W, make Sara sit np. What satisfaction does
it yield a man to be told that “ Sara was simply
immense ” after he had rushed off to catch his
last train ?

Sheridan’s engagement at Melbourne
Royal brought more money into the house than
any other company had drawn since the panto-
mime, or for many months before it. The Firm
accordingly expressed their appreciation by a
testimonial performance in aid of the Widdy last
Monday night, when the curtain finally descended
on their lease. And this reminds us that times
and castes have changed in Melbourne. When
“ Fun on the Bristol ” was first produced at the
Royal, some eight years ago, the press rose up
almost in a body to declare that such a respectable
temple of the drama was desecrated by a pro-
duction so altogether frivolous and “ un-English.”
By the way, Sheridan’s benefit was a huge success,
and the Widdy received many recalls.
At Poverty Point.
Anyone connected with Australian provincial theatricals
will know the Loving Couple, married these 15 years, and
positively you never see one without the other, so that
they should certainly win the Thespian flitch of bacon.
Remember that Bernhardt’s scenery is only paper—the
only paper in the house.
What is Nellie Stewart doing in London? It was an-
nounced before she left, that a definite offer of an
engagement had been made, too good to be refused, but
now everything seems uncertain. Could it have been
only a voyage on spec, after all ? However this may be,
a first appearance at the fag-end of the season, when all
theatre-going London is holiday-making, will hardly give
the young Australian prinia donna a fair show.
That it is the duty of the ticket-collectors at the back
to lead the applause is well recognised at some theatres.
During Charles Warners last season at Melbourne Royal,
when he was playing to poor houses and apathetic
audiences, the “ eminent” one rushed out one night to
the acting-manager. “What the blank dash do you
mean, Mr. ? There’s six of you blank blank dash
fools in front and I never got a hand.”
Melbourne architects complain of “ Billy” Pitt sending
in his Opera House tender with the motto “Princess.”
They urge that it was equivalent to giving his name.
The death of Harry Edwards was reported the other
day, and now a rumour goes round that Olive Berkeley,
his little Lord Fauntleroy, has died after an operation for
her throat-complaint.

The real reason of Elton’s re-engagement. The “ Firm*’
wanted George Walton to take the former’s place, but,
leaving the offer too late, found that the latter had
arranged to take out a company of his own and would
not renew. Which means an extra ten pounds per week
to the great E. The difficulty of finding anyone to re-
him place was also probably the real reason of the unusual
but well-deserved benefit given to Ryley.
Reports to the contrary notwithstanding, it is probable
that H. H. Vincent will never return to Australia. The
days of thumping salaries from the “ Firm ” appear to be
over, and Vincent—who has money—is too good a man
ever to lack an engagement on the London boards.
Taken all round he was, perhaps, the best “old man”
actor Australia has ever seen, and, as Sir Peter Teazle,
was certainly superior to Farren, who in England has a
monopoly of the part. On the other hand, there are
ominous signs that, after a few more spasmodic appear-
ances in melodrama, Charles Warner will once more seek
refuge here.
Maud Williamson has not retired definitely from the
stage, but, after a rest, will probably re-appear in an
original drama.
The somewhat dingy scenery of the Bernhardt season i 8
characteristic of most French plays, and, in spite of what
the gushing critics who haven’t been there may say, the
“setting” is never strong in any Parisian theatre.
Most countries, Australia included, can give the French
points in this respect.
Subscription in Melbourne for G. R. Ireland, the
veteran. His first appearance, as a regular actor, was in
1858, as Sir Eglamour, in the “Two Gentlemen of
Verona,” to the Valentine of G. V. Brooke, the Proteus
of Robt. Heir, the Sylvia of Ellen Mortyn, and the Julia
of Mrs. Heir—Eheu ! As they say in the classics. Before
that he was the crack amateur of Melbourne, with the
Garrick Club, as Claude Melnotte, and that style of thing
- even now, he is not much older than Irving.

France is most ungrateful to its old and used-up
actresses. The great Mdllo Georges, idol of Napoleon’s
era, applied for the caretakershi-p of umbrellas at the
Paris Exhibition, 1857, under Nap. 111., and Bernhardt
once got a nasty jar when she asked a woman, connected
with the theatre, what she, the great Sa-hara, would come
to. “ Well,” says the other, “ you’ll be a box-opener.”
Six new scenes were painted for “Theodora,” two by
Goatcher, two by Hennings, two by Grist. A mighty
refreshment after the faded old stuff.
New York Hkralb has been starting a symposium on
“ Ought Actors to Keep Single,” the contributors being
actors and actresses. We suppose this emanates from the
interviewer who went round to ask the ladies, “ How do
you feel in trousers?” Miss Rhea insists that an actor
ought not to marry an actress, and others contra. Mr.
and Mrs. Kendal get on very nicely, but our memory
is rather short, and, so far as it serves us, actors and
actresses should never marry anyone, not even one
another.
Williamson and G., after freezing out the billstickers
over Bernhardt, did not deign in any shape to publish
the cast of “Theodora.”
Pitt, who designed the Melbourne Princess’, also designs
the new Melbourne Opera House, which, at a cost of
£50,000, will be the most magnificent Thespian temple
south of the line. The house will consist of dress-circle,
stalls, upper-circle, and gallery—three tiers, but what
becomes of the good old pit ? The Royal management
had to restore it, after abolition. There will be a revolving
stage, so that scenes can be set without any intervals be-
tween the acts, as at the theatre Steele Mackaye opened
in New York, where the stage had two floors. It was
hoisted up and down, the set for Act 2 being prepared,
underneath, while Act 1 was going on.
Many actresses who do not draw well paint most
cleverly.
Williamson and Pollard are organising a juvenile troupe
to do the provinces.

Overheard at one of the Melbourne theatres (not the
Princess’, you may be sure) the other night. Canvasser
for daily newspaper to friend: “I’ve only brought the
missus and her mother and sister to-night. To-morrow
night I’ll bring the family.” And he did—seven of them,
and all “ on the nod.”
