The journalistic delusion is that things can be described
beforehand, but never can it be done. “Theodora” is
finished at a good deal after midnight, and a critic pre-
tends that he turns out two columns of most thoughtful
and elaborate copy in time for the morning press!
Nellie Farren makes us think of Julia Matthews. How
her “Sweet Spirit Hear My Prayer,” in “Aladdin,” used
to collar the mashers who are now grand-dads! She
married one Mumford, in Maoriland. It is quite a stage
tradition how her father and mother used to keep her
chained up, the old father in particular, with his weather
eye ori her, and Burke, the explorer, got few opportunities
for his courting.
“ The Cleopatra attire displays Sara’s figure closely. It
is somewhat of manly beauty, with the appearance of
strong chest, rather than bust, though the haunch is
feminine in broad outline ; and, without any suspicion of
ungraeefulness, one likens the set of the trunk on the
understandings to the Hottentot women, whose infants
stand up behind.” Thus much from that esteemed scien-
tific measurer, Dr. Roddrule. Again: “ Her finely-
moulded, long octopussy arms are distinctively feminine.”

In one scene of an Amerioan faroical comedy hangs a
picture of President Harrison whose whiskers are violently
agitated by a rushing noisy wind whenever a door in its
vicinity is opened.
Harry Rickards found some difficulty in getting exactly
what he wanted in America. He has, however, engaged
a company to arrive early next year. Meantime, he goes
to London to fetch out a strong variety 00.
Sybil Sanderson, the American soprano, so says a
leading London paper, can get the upper G in the treble
clef—a note higher than Nilsson or Jenny Lind, though
her voice has not the quality of either singer. Sybil’s
father was Attorney-General of Calfornia.
There was some fun going to England with Toole & Co.«
in the Victoria. Nelly Stewart fainted, owing to tight-
lacing, at the masquerade ball, and Musgrove and
Plumpton carried her to bed. Muzzy’s attempts to be
funny in a comic dress were pitiable. A certain eminent
individual, whose giddiness has been well advertised, was
seriously indisposed during the voyage, and a subscrip-
tion-list was sent round on behalf of his wife.

George Lee writes to say that people are no smarter in
London than in Sydney. He says that when the bar-
keeps can overcharge anyone 2d for a drink that they go
in a corner to tickle themselves. Also that all the latest
London shows are good and well staged. George is glad
that he followed the fortunes of Toole.
To advertise a play at Hobart, Messrs. Gerald and Duff
sent a chain-gang of prisoners through the streets in
charge of mock-constables. Next day a deputation of
indignant citizens waited on Premier Fyah to protest
against the barbarous conduct of the gaol authorities.

Capt. Thrussell, assistant-harbourmaster, Melb., was
the fond possessor of a pet ourang-outang till a short
time since, when it bit him, and he had it assassinated by

A number of the Adelaide nobility declined to patronise
the Bernhardt boom, and absented themselves from the
sale of seats, presumably because they disapproved of the
auction system. Others, who held the same view, also
absented themselves from the sale, but repented, and had
to pay the speculators additional prices for tickets,
later on.
Ellen Terry boasts that she can, at will, turn pale a 8
death before a glass, or blush a rosy red. Very likely. It
is the cleverest woman who is the most deceitful.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The news that though Slavin decisively beat
Kilrain bets were declared off owing to a dispute as to
whether Slavin had scored a knock-out dr a win,
being interpreted, probably means that the referee had
more money on Kilrain than he oared about losing.
Possibly the influential party at the fight had their money
on Kilrain, in just the same way as the influential party at
the Miller-Foley match here some years back, had their
money on the wrong man. Slavin’s record is now cer-
tainly the best of any man’s iri the ring at the present
time, and if he meets Jackson the negro will go under,
sure. When Slavin made his first appearance in Sydney
Tub Bulletin stated its opinion that when he met Jackson
the latter would suffer defeat. This idea brought forth a
lot of indignant letters from readers, ridiculing the notion
as preposterous. Jackson was a little god at the time.
If they met now the betting would be six to four on
Slavin. No man who has seen Slavin at work and marked
the terrific strength of his hitting, and the determined
way in which he bores in after his opponent, would
hesitate to back him against the weak-ribbed, herring-
gutted nigger, Peter Jackson, clever though the latter be.
And how about Goddard? There is no knowing quite
how good Joseph is. He is probably at hds best now, and
if Slavin has been playing any tricks with his constitution
he will find the Barrier Miner a desperately hard man to
dispose of. But if Slavin can go into the ring fit and
strong as he was when he knocked Dooley out -with his
first hit (the writer paid 10s. to see that fight, anditlasted
a minute and a half)—if he can go in as fit as he was then,
he can beat Goddard at his own game. But it would be a
lovely fight, dear bottlenosed readers, a lovely fight.
* * #
Carbine, notwithstanding Donald Wallace’s
arguments to the contrary, is far better in the Melbourne
Cup than either Vengeance or Dreadnought. It was a
foregone conclusion that the champion would be the best
handicapped horse in the race “on paper.” Barnard
couldn’t possibly weight him up to his last year’s form
with Highborn, Vengeance and others, because 12st. fibs,
wouldn’t have been too much for that, and as for Dread-
nought-well, taking a line through Melos, he has the
worst of it with the big horse. All the same, it is absurd
to suppose that Carbine can go on winning Melbourne
Cups for ever, and unlikely that we shall see him turned
out again in the same condition as last November.
# # *
A recently-published book of sporting prattle,
called “ Racing for Gold,” professes to give a new story
about Fred. Archer. It is to the effect that somebody
sent him a parcel containing 100 sovereigns on the night
before the Leger. In the parcel was a note stating that
another 100 “ quid” would follow if he didn’t win the race
next day. Archer was to vide the favourite for Lord
Falmouth, so he went round to his iudship and requested
that another jockey might be put up in his (Archer’s)
stead. But Lord Falmouth said, “ Certainly not, Archer,
you ride the horse and keep the money,” or some lie of
that sort. Therefore, Archer rode the horse, and,
beaten on the post by a short head, he was solaced
by the receipt of another parcel of sovereigns, enclosing a
request “not to draw it quite so fine anothertime.” This
yarn is hopelessly idiotic * on the face of it. The idea of
anyone trying to square the wealthiest jockey of his time
with £lOO, for a race on which he would stand to win
fifty times as much, is about as likely as Archer’s asking
to be taken off the horse. Besides, the present w r riter is
prepared to swear that Archer won the Leger on each
first favourite he rode for Lord Falmouth, and never was
beaten by a short head in the race at all.
# # #
Thiß is a true story about Mr. George Seale, the
well-known N.S.W. amateur boxer. In his rowing days
(he was an intercolonial eight-oar man), Mr. Seale used to
go up and down the Parramatta River a good deal. One
night going up in a late steamer there were a couple of
clumsy roughs on board who began to make themselves
offensive to the passengers—using foul language. Mr.
Seale asked them to stop it, and as he is a medium-sized,
slender, quiet-looking man, they both turned on him,
and wanted to know what the blank blank he had to do
with it, and said that if he didn’t something hold his jaw
they’d make him, and so on. One of them volunteered
the statement that Mr. Seale was wearing a rotten bad
hat. Mr. Seale took off the old soft felt hat he was wear-
ing and gave it a blow on the top. “ Yes,” he said, “ it
shuts up very quickly, and so will you if you don’t watch
yourselves.” His confident attitude rather quietened
them, but they said that if he would only get off at
Abbotsford they’d take the shine out of him. Now, it so
happened that Abbotsford was the very wharf where he
meant to land, so he agreed, and when the wharf was
reached he got out after them, and four or five stalwart
rowing men with him to see fair play. “ Now,” he said,
“ you had a lot to say on the boat—l’ll fight you one after
the other or both together, whichever you like.” But
they couldn’t suffer it. They ran off as hard as they could
split,! one remarking to the other as they went: “ Blime,
Billy, he must be a ’ot member.”

* * •
Long-armed, nuggetty old Dave Ingram, erst-
while N.S.W. champion light-weight—in his day a real
gritty member of the raw-meat bruising school—“passed
over” last week. Since the death of his generous friend,
Jem Punch, Ingram had gone downhill fast. There is a
legend that he was once matched against Sayers with
a fair prospect of success, but was forced by oircumstances
to make a hurried trip to Australia ere the day appointed
for the fight.
* * *
A rather hard-up-looking young man, sitting on
the Pastoral Hotel water-trough (Flemington), was the
other day asked by a Johnnie” to keep his heye on
his ’oss and he would leave him a long beer in the bar.
Little did that Johnnie think when he made this gener-
ous offer that the man on the trough once, and not so
many years ago, either, had more thousands than he ever
had pounds, and could have a special train to cart his
string of racehorses about and laugh when he went down
for a few thousands. And yet he was spoken of as a
“ knowing fellow,” and a word dropped from him caused
many a horse to go up in the market. He played against
the ring and lost. And when “ broke ” he had the bitter
pleasure of seeing from the Hill a nag that had once
lost him a fortune, win one of our greatest events. How
those cheers must have grated upon that hard-up one’s
ears! But now, Johnnie will take your long ’un. In
those days he was worth a solid £lOO,OOO, and now he sits
wearily about the hotels !

Sullivan easily beat Dutch in a wager boat-race,
over the Parramatta course. With 5 secs, start, he
covered the distance in the marvellous time of 18 thin.
41J secs., as against the previous record, by Searle, of
19 min. 52J secs. Everything, of course, was in his favour
a strong ebb and a flow of fresh, and a Westerly breeze.
The first mile was put up in 5 min. 19 secs. Sullivan —
an 11-s(one man—will probably be matched for the
championship.

The daily press vigorously urged the
N.S.W. strikers to adopt “ constitootional ”
methods and have their grievances redressed by
Parliament. Well, they have adopted “consti-
tootional ” methods, and have now returned as
their nominees over a-fourth of the whole House.
Still the daily papers aren’t satisfied.
“ The infant daughter of the Duke and
Duchess of Fife has been christened in the Chapel
Royal with water specially brought from the
river Jordan.” Why should this greasy blasphemy
be cabled to Australia, and thrust into the faces
of the public ? It quite spoilt our breakfast.
That level-headed Scotchman, Hon. James
Service, writing in the Melbourne daily press
strongly counsels the pastoralists to confer with
the unionists. “We have arrived at ‘ the parting
of the way.’ A little kindly feeling and just con-
sideration on the part of the employers now, and
the clouds will part, and the sun will shine again
on ‘ the land we love.’ A mistake now, and what
‘ clouds and darkness ’ will rest upon our future
no man can tell.”
Dan. O’Connor, who quietly relied, no
doubt, on “the Oirish vote,” has been “left ” for
Monaro. There is no longer any solid Irish vote,
as the Irishmen—who have absolutely nothing to
thank Daniel for—-have, in the main, become good
Australians, who in politics know not any man’s
country. The votes of Dawson and Miller, the
elected men, two of the cleanest men in the
Assembly, can always be relied upon in any
honest democratic cause.
According to Brisbane Courier certain
bones passed along by the executors of H. C.
Hartman, of Toowoomba—Col. Olcott’s benefac-
tor, by the way—prove that not only the moa but
the kiwi or apterix, formerly had its home in Aus-
tralia. We take this with salt. There has been
too much swindling done in “ rock footprints of
the moa ” to make us go in for anything less than
a Maori mummy, and the fact that the informa-
tion comes through the superstitious and yet
faddily scientific Hartmann (nowin Nirvana) asks
us to increase this order to a fossil Mahatma at
the very least.
A Sydney daily newspaper correspondent
suggests an alternative method to the £4O deposit
for securing the bona-Jides of Parliamentary can-
didates. He would “ substitute for the £4O, at the
option of the candidate a nomination paper signed
by a specified proportion of the electors on the
roll—say 5 per cent. Such signatures should
be considered as votes given to the candidate and
counted in the subsequent poll, and the signers
should be not allowed to vote again at such elec-
tion, unless more than one member were required to
be elected. This hypothecation of the votes of
the nominators would afford sufficient guarantee
that a fair proportion of the electors were pre-
pared to support their nominee, and constitute a
safeguard against practical jokes or frivolous
nominations.” This looks very plausible, but it
wouldn’t do. It would, in (a measure, abolish the
secrecy of the ballot, inasmuch as creditors could
bring pressure on their debtorsHo sign nomina-
tions for them. Money could, in fact, be lent on
or given for votes with the certainty of delivery.

A cousin of Jerome K. Jerome is a doctor
practising in Victoria, and it was his father who
gave Jerome the first start at publishing his
works. He says that although the family always
thought the author a very decent fellow, their
unanimous opinion of the works in manuscript
was that they were ghastly rubbish.

Rev. Adam Turnbull, M;D.* of Tasmania,
who died the other day, aged 88, was at one time
Colonial Treasurer. Toward the close Of his poli-
tical Career lie eloquently moved a resolution to
tile effect that transportation to Tasmania should
thehceforth cease. Next morning he was cashiered
and excluded from all tlife offices which he had
hitherto held from the Government. He appealed
to the British authorities and was re-instated in
Some of these, but immediately made ah honour-
able resignation, and shortly after retired to the
caliber atmosphere of the Church of Scotland;

“Schoolmaster” Buffet, long of Pitcairn
Island, has just died, aged 94, at Norfolk Island,
ft was in 1822 that the whaler Cyrus called in at
Pitcairn for food, and the Pitcairners asked for a
schoolmaster in exchange. Young Buffett
volunteered, and remained with the descendants
of John Adams for nearly 70 years. He used to
do a big trade in ornamental cabinet and filigree
work, always giving out the same venerable cram
—that the wood was from the old house of John
Adams. For the last 18 months of his life, the
patriarch was imbecile.
Salomons, Q.C., got off a bettor joke than
usual last week in an action against the N.S.W.
Railway Commissioners for injury to person. A
medical witness (not a fashionable sawbones) was
giving evidence for plaintiff, and stated that the
last time he attended him was on Bth March.
When giving the date, he referred to a pencil-
mark on his shirt-cuff. Salomons screamed an
objection that lie should only refresh his memory
from a book, in which the date had been noted at
the time. Witness: “I refreshed my memory
from the book about a week ago.” Salomons :
“ Ori the same cuff ? ”

Talk Of fancy floricultureA recent
cablegram states that a Siliila doctor has siicceeded
iii growing two specimens of leprosy bacillus Hi

A Victorian contemporary prints the fol-
lowing statement without comment: —“ Henry
George says that, in the Australian colonies there
are many little indications of that spirit which, if
suffered to grow and intensify, may give justifica-
tion to the adage that, ‘peoples separated by
creeds may more bitterly hate each other than
those separated by oceans.’” This is a pot-shot
criticism that has missed its mark. Anybody
who has seen the St. Patrick’s Day and Twelfth of
July celebrations out here well know how much
truth there is in it. In the North of Ireland
there are still hostile demonstrations that seriously
incline to wind-up in the infirmary ; here they are
merely al fresco pantomimes, whose worst results-
are confined to a few headaches, and a vast
amount of flatulency, induced by reckless con-
sumption of aerated waters. The only people who
entertain even a mild dislike of each other on
religious—or rather irreligious—grounds, in this
country, are those who have no creed, but dis-
believe differently. Parsons, presbyteries and
city missionaries must introduce a lot more
asceticism into their manner of living before they
will be able to get their congregations to hate
each other over points of doctrine with the old-
time fervour.
“No man is a prophet in his own country'
is an old saw that has lately been proved to
have a broken back. We acknowledge we do
not see many instances of men and things pos-
sessing great reputations at the home of their
birth; but with the proprietor of Clements
Tonic the case is just the reverse of what the
old adage says. This article is consumed in
large quantities right at the seat of its manu-
facture just as much as at places remote there-
from, and this patronage is accorded to it on
account of its genuine, acknowledged, and un-
disputed merit; because Clements Tonic is not
a quack nostrum, but a reliable, scientific
specific, antagonistic to diseased conditions.
Most diseases are due to a disordered state of
the blood which becomes contaminated. Disease
thrives on these impurities and spreads through-
out the system and ends in fatal disease,
Clements Tonic so purifies the blood and re-
vitalizes it, that diseased germs cannot exist,
Clements Tonic regenerates the entire system :
it contains all material which brain and physical
w-orkers consume in their respective avoca-
tions ; —as one employment weakens the body
Clements Tonic replaces in the blood the con-
stituents consumed, which restore the healthy
condition, and this is the secret of its great suc-
cess. Not because it is a cure-all, but because
it treats the root of disease and not the branches.

Sydney aldermen in the N.S.W. Legislative

The Bulletin predicts the early disappear
ance of the bell topper from Australian political life-
The white-waistcoat will go with it, and so will
the lawyers and the commission-agents who have
hitherto monopolised the seats in the Legislatures.

Well did Hancock, in the Victorian Assem-
bly bring out The Bulletin’s point about the
dead silence in which the troops marched through
Melbourne on Queen Victoria’s birthday. He
grasped the nettle by declaring that the people
were silent because they knew the rifles were
intended for them.
The just and sensible “ betterment '’ prin-
ciple is steadily gaining ground. It is now pro-
posed that the long-promised bridge between
Svdney and its future Brooklyn, North Shore,
shall be constructed by taxing those whose land
would be improved by such a bridge—payments
to extend over a period of 100 years.
Many people bewail the fact that a lot
of recent N.S.W. members —“ men with a stake
in the country ’’—have been rejected in favour of
labour candidates. “ Anarchy is coming ! ” shriek
these Sydney Jeremiahs in chorus with Maeßain.
“Anarchy be hanged ! ” the other day said a level-
headed shop-keeper who had supported the labour
men. “Why, who are the rejected men whom
you are weeping about ? When I used to buy fish
on the wharf, one or two of them used to come
there to buy pigs; and it’s only a year or two
since the biggest man amongst them was fined for
killing calves in his back-yard.”
Charles Pratt, the American twenty-mil-
lionaire, who died suddenly the other day, was
a devout Christian, a generous giver to charitable
objects, and an active Standard Oil monopolist.
This, remarks the Twentieth Century, is a com-
bination of characteristics that makes a person
one of the “fittest” in this day and generation.
He made the most of both worlds.

continue to move so wisely the four millions of Austra-
lians will establish a federation which will be a glory to

B D. Morehoad, now starring it on his
wealth, in London, as a prominent Queenslander,
suggests further immigration to the northern
colony as a cure for the labour troubles. More-
head is a squatter of the squatters, and, though a
jolly good mate, is an odious master. His pro-
posal is really a cure for fair wages, and means
the re-establishment of labour slavery. _ After the
labour troubles are cured will be the time tor all
the further immigration, so long as it is of a
healthy kind, needed in Australia. Till then, the
land can do well enough without “cheap labour ”
immigrants.

The Victorian Assembly has 95 members
for 243,730 electors, or, roughly, one member for
2565 voters ; Queensland, 72 members for 73,957
electors, or one member for little more than 1000
voters ; South Australia, 54 members for b 9,873
electors, one for 1200 voters ; Tasmania, 36 mem-
bers for 26,054 electors, one for 890 voters ; JN.b.
Wales in her last Parliament had 137 members
for 298,039 electors, or one for 2175.
A Reverend somebody has been promising
the Sydney unemployed 255. a week, rations and
free railway passes if they’ll take station work.
More Governmental interference on behalf of
monopoly and the Employers’ Union. Decent
men and poor who have country billets to go to
are systematically refused passes, while the man
who will undersell his labour as a shearer or
shearers’ assistant, can ride free anywhere.
Hancock, the labour-member elected for
Collingwood despite the opposition of the Age,
made a splendid splash in the Victorian Assembly
in moving the reply to the address. Casting to
the winds the time-honoured gabble over the
points provided for him, he launched into the real
questions of the day, boldly taking the first stand
for Labour ever thus made in an Australian or
any other Parliament. How is it Trenwith has
been all this time in Parliament, missing the
chance Hancock seized.'
Mr. Carruthers, N.S.W. Minister for
Public Instruction, has issued an address, grate-
fully thanking the “7231 electors who voted for
him at the poll.” Why-doesn’t Minister Cai-
ruthers thank the 4500 gentlemen or so, and the
2700 allotments or so, who voted for him on the
occasion? Such thanks always recall the good
old story told of Benjamin Franklin, who once
said, speaking of the property-vote system, that a
friend of his had in the morning an ass and with
it a vote, but at mid-day the ass died, and then
his friend had no vote— ergo, it was the ass and not
tlie man to whom the vote had beiontted MUnister
Carruthers numbers among his political trien
many men with many asses.

The saddest spectacle ever seen in the
annals of a broken-down institution was the last
attempt of the Victorian Freetrade Association to
raise the wind and postpone the inevitable
collapse. It was about the last meeting of that
ruined circus, and the hall was nearly empty, and
the fire was out in the stove, and the gas was dim,
and everything was flat, wasted, and unprofitable.
Then somebody rose uv> and told the audience that
a gentleman on the platform had promised a
guinea, provided 19 other gentlemen gave 1J
guineas among them as a preliminary, and under
these circumstances he urged the 19 gentlemen to
rush forward and thus enable the society to profit
by the liberality of the gentleman on the plat-
form. There weren’t 19 gentlemen present, but
all that were there got up on the instant, and went
straight out without contributing a cent., and the
lavish person on the platform stuck to the exact
terms of his offer, and didn’t put up a solitary
copper.

There died the other day at Ararat (Vic.)
Hospital an illustrative old remnant of the convict
system—William Chaffey, aged 91, who reached
Vandemonia as a transportee in 1818. His corpse
still revealed signs of the many floergings he had
undergone. Chaffey was once one of six who
stood under the gallows, but was with two others
reprieved when the noose was round his neck.
A cheeky Rockhampton offender, “ lum
bered ” for larceny, took his revenge on the Court
by talking over his case—a simple one, which
could have been fairly settled in three hours—for
three solid days. Judge Miller wearily allowed
him rope, but the jurymen, who were locked up
for three nights, would have liked to screw his
neck. They didn’t wait three seconds, when the
end came, as to their verdict, which was delivered
with a vicious steel-trap snap, and the Judge gave
him “seven years” with a loud click. Seven
years for talking—we mean larceny 1

Concurrently with the news that the S.A.
Parliament has voted money to buy Government
sites for workmen’s dwellings, near Adelaide*
comes the news that the Queensland Government
meditate buying two large estates on the Darling
Downs, to be paid for in Government debentures
at par, payable in 25 years, and bearing per
cent, interest. The price named is £3 an acre tor
150,000 acres, which will be readily taken by
selectors at a price to fully cover ad outlay.
Other landowners are willing to make similar
terms. This is a neat way of “ fleeing from the
wrath to come.”
This is cutting things tolerably fine. A
woman named King was travelling in a Melbourne
tramcar, and suddenly became excited a,t seeing a
timber*waggon on the line right ahead. .Being
under the impression that she was going to be
squashed in a heartrending collision, she clutched
the rail of the car with such tremendous energy
that she injured her constitution, and brought on
“neuralgic paralysis.” The hind-wheel of the
waggon did graze the tramcar, though it didn t
hit anywhere near the spot where the excited
female was holding on; but, all the same, she sued
for damages, and .fudge Hamilton gave Mbo.
This was very agreeable for her, no doubt,
but it was desperately rough on the de-
fendant, owner of the waggon. Ihe latter
is also a female—a lone woman who makes
an honest livelihood in the firewood business, and
she is let in for some £3OO in damages and costs,
because a stranger was scared at the sight of her
cart and kinked her spine through clutching a
tramcar when there was no earthly necessity for
it. And now the question arises—how tar can
this sort of game be carried ? The plaintiff was
allowed damages because there was a collision,
but if the other vehicle had got clear she would
have been injured just the same, for she wasn t
damaged by the collision, but by hauling in an
insane manner at the tramway company s pio-
perty The situation would have been just tne
same if she had been scared at the sight of a dog
as she came round a corner, and had hung on to a
lamp-post till she had broken her back, and then
sued the dog’s owner for damages. Or she might-
have been travelling by train, and, seeing Parkes
through the window, she might have shrieked till
she fractured both her legs, and after that Parkes
would have been liable. The Judge pointed out
that she ought to get the money because she was
injured “in trying to take care of herself, but a
woman who takes care of herself by grabbing a
tramcar and getting paralysed is altogether too
original for this world. Truly the law is a large
variety of mules.
Scientific Advertising.
Many are the theories entertained by unsophisti-
cated minds upon this subject, the greater part of
them being wide of the mark as to its policy,
influence, and advantages. Modern advertising
has become so much a scientific pursuit that the
old conditions are altogether changed, not merely
elaborated and systemnised, but laid down on a
newly devised basis, upon which is raised a super-
structure at once elegant and impressive. A
simple announcement in as few words as possible
(to save expense) regarding some article of ordin-
ary economical value and domestic use, is now
scarcely regarded by the purchasing public. Io
be effective and arrest attention nowadays, the
article must possess some advantages hitherto not
specifically attributed to it, which may refer
either to quality or quantity ; and the display o
these new principles in conspicuous and attractive
characters of the printers’or lithographers arts,
constitute one very marked alteration m modern
advertising. This leads to the point regarding
peculiarities in the styles practised by different
houses; while some are altogether for pictorial
illustrations, others follow by a happy medium
between the lithographic and plain typographical
arts. One celebrated patent medicine company
have long adopted the paragraph style of news-
paper advertising, whereby the article under con-
sideration is covertly and adroitly introduced by
a happy allusion to some topic of the day or hour,
finally securing the design intended by the writer
in the climax. This system possesses only one
drawback in the case of a reader who is antagon-
istic to the views put forth, which may produce
an anathema upon the author of the dissimulation.
But the great secret in extensive and successful
advertising lies in the fact that the article set
forth for the admiration of the people must be a
really good thing in itself, not a one-bottle remedy
for every disease; and if it has already withstood
the test of time and obtained the confidence of a
large proportion of the community, so much the
better its chance of successfully attracting the
notice of general readers. Upon these premises we
venture to name as the objects worthy of wide-
world advertising Warner’s Safe Cure for liver
and urinary diseases. Warners Safe Diabetes
Cure, Warner’s Safe Rheumatic Cure, Warner s
Safe Pills, the only known specifics for the several
complaints for which they are indicated. The
following is the latest certificate received from a
patient cured of diabetes: —' Camden, N.S.W.,
19th May, 1891.—1 t gives me grea-t pleasure m
testifying to the great benefit I derived from the
use of your Safe Diabetes Cure. I suffered for
years with sugar diabetes, and was brought very
low in consequence. Having lost severa pounds
in flesh I had not sufficient strength to walk across
the yard My excessive thirst was almost unbear,
able, great weakness and pains down my legs, and
mv urine contained a very large amount of suga r ,
I was told I could not possibly recover. I would
not have cared to live but for my wife and family
I tried many remedies and consulted seveial
doctors to no benefit, I was persuaded against my
will to try Warner’s “Safe” Diabetes Cure, l
procured a case, and commenced its use, and co n .
tinued to get better to the surprise of myself an d
friends. lam now able to go about my busines
(Signed) JAS. JESSUP,
Station Manager for Hon. C. Dangar, M.D.A.

SOCIETY
The S. M. Herald’s dramatic critic
congratulates the Sydney public on Sara Bern-
hardt’s compliment to their taste in producing an
original and yet unacted play while in Sydney.
What’s wrong with our old friend, ‘ ‘ Experimcntum
pat in corpore vili ” ?
* * *
Sir Patrick Jenkins—beg pardon, Jennings
—who was already an M.L.G., K.C.M.G., and
LL.D., has now been made by the Pope a here-
ditary Marquis of the Holy Roman Empire. And
as there is no Holy Roman Empire just now, the
distinction amounts to what the Germans call
“ nix.” Besides, there is no one to call him Mar-
quis, except derisively, in this beastly country—if
you except the little coterie of gilded publicans
and sich already “ distinguished ” by His Howli-
ness, who solemnly greet each other with “ How
do you do, Sir John ?” and so on at Church meet-
ings. The Marquis Jennings! Booh ! It is
stated that this distinction is intended by the
Pope as “an honour to the colony ofN.S.W.”
As the Pope has not provided enough Marquis to
go all round the 1,100,000 people comprising the
N.S.W. population, the distinction had better be
divided amongst and confined to the Marquis
Jenkins’ fellow-members of the Legislative Coun-
cil, the 68 members of which are hereby declared
by The Bulletin to be each the 68th part of a
Marquis of the Holy Roman Empire. Augustus,
the microscope, please.
# # #
The Melbourne pressmen, who have not
hitherto been notorious for emotional effects,
seemed to lead the tumult at Sara’s farewell,
scene which is not likely to be repeated in a Mel
bourne theatre for a long time to come. It had
happened, however, that Theodora’s death—ti e
finishing touch to an unusually earnest perfor
ance —was spoilt by an accident to her back-hair.
As the executioner advanced, Sara observed that
she had a plait dangling over her shoulder, and at
that supreme moment she quietly pulled out a
hairpin and skewered the unfortunate hair into a
place of safety. In fact, Sara had hold of her hair
when the executioner twisted the cord round her
neck. This presence of mind is said to be one of
Sara’s strongest points. She can shed real tears
of anguish whilst surreptitiously arranging any
little thing that has gone wrong.
Another Adelaide parson has gone wrong.
He came out to the sacred city some time ago and
left a young wife and child in England. His
congregation thought him single, and the parson,
who didn’t deny the impeachment, soon began to
cultivate the acquaintance of a lady-member of
the congregation. Matters became lively, and the
clergyman booked his passage for England. The
lady’s father heard of the projected departure,
and hied himself to the vessel’s cabin, where he
found the frisky parson chanting “Abide with
me,” &c., with a “cat” congregation. That
father became indignant, and gave his reverence a
K 1 hiding in the presence of the passengers,
reverend one is now on the way to England.
# # *
A young man named Lawrence M. David-
son was introduced to a number of good Adelaide
families “ principally connected with the church
which he hadjoined,andof which he showed himself
a zealous member. ” Presently he married the elder
Of two sisters of good social position, and very
speedily ran away by mail-steamer with the
younger, who was only 18 years of age. Mr.
Davidson, at whose door charges of false-pretences
ate laid, and who is to be arrested when the mail
reaches Colombo, seems a very fair sample of the
“zealous church-member” turned out in such
large numbers just now. The Devil may now
and then miss a prayer-meeting, but he never
stays away from church.
# # *
A London journalist advises those who
wish for unlimited credit and the confidence of
the financial world to smoke eight-and-sixpenny
cigars. We have improved on that in Australia.
A white choker, a sanctimonious smirk' and a
Sunday-school reputation are cheaper and far
taore efficacious.
* * *
When Dan. O’Connor accompanied Lady
Jersey to Newcastle he begged of a well-known
literary official in the local District Court to bring
his lecture on Daniel Deniehy under the notice of
the Countess on her visit to the local School of
Arts. Entreaties and demands, however, failed,
as H. W. H. H. insisted on calling attention to
his own literary contributions, much to Daniel’s
chagrin and Lady Jersey’s amusement.
# # *
Weird stories are told of the doings of a
certain theatrical company ( not Sara’s) on the
voyage to Barataria. One fascinating creature
and her swain were, by the captain’s orders,
washed off deck with the hose at 3 a.m. And an
important official, not connected with the naviga-
tion department, was silently put ashore at
Colombo, and left there, for having been dis-
covered in somebody’s cabin under peculiarly in-
criminating circumstances. Wild horses wouldn’t
drag from us the name of the lady who was igno.
miniously doused, nor of the heroine of the cabin
incident. We merely wish to point out that every
passenger ought to be provided j with a stern,
inflexible maiden aunt; in the interests of the
proprieties. , ~

# * #
Complaints reach The Bulletin, from
several sources, concerning the haughty indolence
of the stipendiary magistrate of a new bush town,
who affects late nights, and often reaches court
at noon, until when a score or two of suitors,
witnesses, &c., await his pleasure. However, he
has been known to run up early when a squatto-
cratie crony wanted Viis “ little case turned off
quietly before the public opening of court.”

A COUNTRY ART-CRITIC.
ONLOOKER: “What might you get for that, guv'nor?”
ARTIST “It depends on whether it sells.”
ONLOOKER: “Oh! I thought it was for a land-sale.”
M.B.M.S. Curacoa has returned to Sydney
“ after a Highly enjoyable cruise ” in N.Z. waters.
Her first exploit on getting into Sydney harbor
was to block up the fairway at night and obstin-
ately refuse to give way to the Manly boat, which
had to leave its right course and squeeze up against
the lightship to avoid a collision. The boat (the
Narrabeen) whistled eight or nine times without
avail: not the ghost of a response came from Her
Majesty’s ship “ Curacoa.” This—let us whisper
it softly in the ear of the Admiral—looks remark-
ably like brandy and Curacoa.
m * *
Police-magistrate Pinnock, of Brisbane,
has decided—Confucius only knows why—that the
money found on any cadaver that is fished out of
the Brisbane River'belongs to the Chief Justice.
Anybody who goes out fishing with the Chief
Justice had better make a note of this, for if he goes
overboard it is to Lilley’s interest to leave him there
—if that judicial authority drags him out he, the
Jedge, will be a distinct loser by the transaction.
People who are sitting on the edge of the wharf at
midnight should also make anote of it, and if heysee
Sir Chas. Lilley coming up behind them on all fours
and'lkeeping as much as possible in the shadow
they ought to rise up and leave the spot with
all haste. Persons who have friends or relations
asphyxiated in the river ought to find out if the:
Judge was anywhere about when the deceased
fell in. Nobody supposes, of course, that the Chief
Justice would voluntarily shove anybody over the
end of a pier for the sake of the perquisites but
still, temptation ought not to be put in any man’s
way.
One of a riertain Australian judicial auto-
crat’s daughters got married without the old
man’s consent. Pa thereupon sent round circulars
j;o all his friends, requestirig them in no way to
recognise the couple. f ,

* # *
Donald Wallace is evidently a legislator of
liberal cash principles. When the Victorian
Council were discussing the propriety of officially
thanking their retired clerk as John Bai-ker Esq.,
rather than plain J. 8., the owner of Carbine
suggested that “ Esq.” wasn’t any practical
evidence qf their friendly feelings towards Barker,
and the best way of showing regard was to put
one’s hand in one’s pocket. Whereupon the rest
of the Council assumed a dull, leaden expression
of countenance, and resumed the debate at the
point where the irreverent Donald had chipped
in. • Ultimately Barker was described as Esq. on
the minutes of the House, to the great satisfaction
of colonel—or is it Field Marshal ? —Softgoods
Sargood, a gentleman who loves to remember that
every knight had an esquire to polish up his tin
suits in days of old.
* # *
It was Zeal, MvP. , who suggested to knock
out the “Esquire” after his name, and others
supported him. This trifle is a sign of the times,
fof they only allowed the “ Esquire ” to stand on
the hypothesis that “ it meant nothing.”
At thh opening of Victorian Parliament,
Gov.' Hopetoun turned out in his imported
carriage, with coachee and three flunkeys in cocked
hats, plastered wigs, Windsor uniforms, or what-
ever they call ’em, knee-breeches, and stockings.
The horses were done up with ribbons, ahd what
riot. Fellah must do something to revive expiring
Loyalty. But such things are only Republican
advertisements!
* * *
Lady Hopetoun carried her arm ill a sling
to the opening of Parliament last week. —
So to our screed,
Tightly we cling —
Last word, indeed,
Rhyming with sling.
* # *
Gov. Hopetoun’s twang gave quite a low-
comedian touch to the end of his speech at last
week’s opening of the Melbourne Row-Wow. Re-
citing a list of bills to be introduced during the
session, the jaw-dropping young man started
people grinning when he arrived at police
offences, thistles, religious and charitable trusts,
insects,” and was rewarded with a general laugh
when he finished up with “boilers and ceme-
teries.” Then Hopetoun looked up plaintively
from under his huge cocked hat and smiled alter
the fashion of a lay-preacher who wonders where
the joke comes in. The joke in this instance was
a simple matter of sound, of the sad and tired
monotone which inevitably sets people thinking
of things like thistles, boilers and cemeteries—
three of the most monotonous institutions on
this weary earth—before the words are spoken.

According to present appearances, Shiels’
Act will, in its first year of operation, quadruple
the number of divorces in Victoria, The success-
ful female petitioners are in a majority of more
than two to one against the husbands.
Item in the Orange (N/S.W.) breach-of-
promise case between a lovely creole (Agnes
Moore) and E. G. Agland -.—She asked him if he
intended marrying her, and he said —“No. I
have a dog, a horse, and a few old clothes ; you
can have them.” “Ted, I don’t want them; I
want you.” And still he bolted.

# * *
Servant-Girls’ Union formed at Charle-
ville (Q.)—Scale of wages adopted : —Cooks, £2 ;
barmaids, £1 55.; generals, 15s. 6d. ; nursegirls,
12s. Gd. All female servants to be allowed out
after 8 p.m. every night. Good. The plain
Mary who does the necessary work of cooking is
properly ranked more highly than the mysteri-
ously-constructed “ lady ” who sells you disguised
kerosene with a smile that makes you swear it’s
prime whisky.

A HARMLESS SLING (OF INE).
Full of alarm,'
Sadly we sing.
Sing of that arm
Laid in a sling.
Pretty young queen
Of a toy king,
Why is she seen
Wearing a sling ?
Is it a burn—
Troublesome thing ?
Truth we would learn
Touching: that sling.
Better have “place”
Come on the wing
Than the whole face
Tied in a sling.
Fighters who fight
Rounds in a ring
Never look right
Out of a sling.
Same with a Chow,
Slanting-eyed Chiiig
Gets in a row,
Make him a sling.
Lady with charms
Fresh as the Spring
Mustn’t have arms .
Bound in a sling.
Think of the check
When she would fling,
Round hubby’s neck
Ann in a sling.
Now to a close
Ballad we bring,
Such you suppose
Easy to sling.
Yet it has got
Rhythmical swing
Certainly not
Feet in a sling.
Whenever lovely woman kneels
(As oft occurs, of course),
She prays aloud for Mister Shiels
Until she’s fairly hoarse.
And should a brutal husband’s heels
Be pressed into her chest she feels
A sense of triumph as she squeals—
“ The Law is my resource !”
Or when the giddy dawg reveals,
In staring eyes and frequent reels,
The presence of a foe that steals
Men’s brains away, or chucks his meals
About the room ’midst laughter-peals, •
That woman takes her woes or wheals
To Justice Nisi, one who heals
Her wounds with balm prepared by Shiels—
The ointment of Divorce.

# # #
Rear Bulletin, —Your paragraph as to the
action of chloroform is correct. I knew Lawrie
ivell, arid am prepared to back his opinion;
besides, attending only to the breathing and
neglecting the pulse, 1 have given it in some
Hundreds of cases without any fatality, and the
only time I ever saw signs of collapse a recourse
to artificial respiration at once restored vitality.
How long will the exploded notion that it is
necessary before giving chloroform to carefully
examine the heart continue to exist in the minds
of coroners, juries and doctors (some)? The
sound rule is that a patient who can stand the
operation can stand the chloroform, and some late
fatal cases may, perhaps, be due to the fact that
the patient could not stand the operation.—Yours,
GfcoRGE Rant.
# # •
A diver employed in salvage operations
After a N.S.W. marine disaster some time ago,
before finally fixing on his helmet, absorbed two'
bottles of beer ; then he went below—and stayed
down for an extraordinary long time without in
any way disturbing or signalling the people above.
His life-line on being pulled was found to be fast.
Something serious being apprehended, another
diver was hurriedly sent below —and found the
loose end of the life-line made fast to a stanchion
under a rock. The air-tube, being followed, led
to a cabin, where the diver was found comfortably
sleeping in a berth. This for coolness “takes
the cake.” We know of no other man who
has ever gone 30 feet below water to enjoy a quiet
snooze.
# # *
One point about the Gordon-Cumming
case, writes a Bulletin correspondent, would
seem not to have been sufficiently comtiiented on,
and that is that the self-appointed band of
Watchers formed, with one exception, a purely
family party, who Were not only the hosts of the
man they watched, but, presumably, knew very
little about baccarat. It is significant that
General Owen Williams and Lord Coventry, both
old hands, were not invited to join the party, but
were only informed of the matter when the game
Was over. This, apart from other more or less
“ fishy ” incidents, throws an air of suspicion over
the whole affair, and should have given Gordon-
Cumming the verdict with any but the most
truckling jury. It would be interesting to know
what Lieutenant Lyvett’s friends think of his
conduct in forming one of a party to watch a
brother officer without taking every precaution
that there should be no mistake about the result.
# * *
A gentleman and lady visited a London
cafe, where the lady began smoking a cigarette.
A waiter politely told her that ladies were not
allowed to smoke there. Whereupon her knight
broke the waiter’s head with a water-bottle.
Summons. Decided that a lady has a right to
smoke wherever a man has a similar privilege;
fined, therefore, Is. only. Whoop for the cigar-
ettistes !

Lord Brooke, it is cabled, lias threatened
criminal proceedings against anyone connecting
the name of Lady Brooke with the incidents
arising out of the late baccarat scandal at Tranby
Croft. Sooner or later we shall find out the name
of the woman who was at the bottom of the
scandal, and how she came there. Perhaps London
society knows now.

From Down and Gumtree Journal :
Mrs. entertained several friends at a theatre
party the other night at “ The Gondoliers,” the occasion
being her birthday. Supper at Gunsler’s afterwards J
champagne in moderation.
This latter clause is significant and may yet
become stereotyped in connection with such noti-
fications. So many more or less fashionable Syd-
ney women and girls have been seen drunk in
public of late, especially after theatre-parties, that
is as well to be explicit.

Mrs. Lucinda Parkinson, the Castlemaine
school-teacher, who used to kneel beside her hus-
band and ask the Lord to take him hence as soon
as possible, h'as been allowed to resign her billet
on a “retiring allowance.” Lucinda can make
things very warm for hubby now, and The Bul-
letin has run up a simple little prayer which
ought to meet her ease to a nicety. Down on
your knees, ’Cinda :
Forty-four pounds is the cost of “ getting
a marriage done to rights ” at Melbourne Anglican
Cathedral. Divorces are cheaper, but not so cheap.
# # #
Melbourne leading “houses ” are reducing
the wages of their chiefer employes —mostly “by
consent,” ’tis said. Yes, that sort of consent that
can’t help itself. That sort of consent which means
—“ If you don’t consent you may go.” They begin
to define “consent” as they define “freedom of
contract.”
# # #
No one at all tainted with “ vulgar ” trade
is considered good enough to be bidden to the Hay
ball in show-week. The invites are to be strictly
confined to commission-agents, sheep-kings, and
Government officials.
# # *
General Tulloch’s address to the penitent
Ballarat regiment smacks of Cambridge’s oration
over the Bermuda Guards. Tulloch declared that
even in distant Queensland he couldn’t sleep o’
nights, sorrowing and weeping over sinful Bal-
larat. Reminds you of that apothecary who sat
up whole nights watching over the British Con-
stitution. The simple fact is the Ballaratarians
had grievances, but found it impossible to
ventilate them save by insubordination. Tul-
loch’s solemnity was ridiculous, and the reports
are read with laughter.
Dear Bulletin, —In a recent issue “ Hugh
K. Liptus ” asks for the meaning of the word
HE ALSO KNEW.
JONES (savagely) : “Do you know that your chickens come into my yard?"
SMITH: “Yes, and I know that they never come out of it again.”
Toowoomba. W. H. Groom (who is M.P. for
Toowoomba and wants to stay so) says it means
“ great in the future,” but a Toowoomba
aboriginal once informed me that the meaning of
the word (which he sounded like “Chookoomba ”)
was “bubbling, or rising water.” The black's
definition is more likely to be correct as Too-
woomba (once known as “Drayton Swamp”) is
surrounded by swamps.—Yours, Swamp.

missal, “is simply a wolf in sheep’s clothing, and
ought to be scrupulously shunned by all suscept-
ible maidens.” There was a larkish episode be
tWeen the missionary and a skittish lamb, and
they even “ stopped up the keyhole.
# # #
Dear Bulletin, —If the world takes more
interest in its bad heroines is not the world wise
in its generation ? Does not beauty fade and do not
good men pall, and is not the virtuous heroine of
one drama as like as two peas to the virtuous
heroine of all other dramas? Can the soulless
lump of clay, whose every action can be predicted
with mathematical accuracy, ever inspire more
than a passing sympathy quickly followed by a
feeling of satiety? It is the “bad un,' m the
purely modern sense of the term, who displays the
capacity for passion and the power of ruthlessly
crushing down inane conventionalities and whose
actions, following the dictates of a strong nature
influenced only by its own impulses and regardless
of the world, lead to the unexpected in both the
real and mimic drama. The only chance for
beauteous virtue is misfortune. But for her end
who would remember Marie _ Antoinette ?—and
that perfect piece of Dresden c ina, me.
Recamier, is quite forgotten. Cora Pearl tiacirea
hair and only the beaute de dia > ,
heroine of the Second Empire could command the
same homage. Moral :It is the unknown which
attracts, and plain women rule the world.—Yours,
George Dant.
* * #
Something that looked like the beginning
of a social trouble took place the other night in a
Melbourne theatre. A party consisting of two
gentleman and two ladies came in together, and
sat down in the circle. During the performance
one of theladies dropped her fan under the seat,
and she and the two gentlemen all reached down
after it at the same moment. One of them, how-
ever, reached so violently that his waistcoat burst
open, and a letter fell down beside the fan, and
the lady picked the two articles up together.
Then she glanced at the document -the peop e
in the seat behind noticed that it was in a temaie
handwriting—and as she did so an expression of
petrified ferocity spread over her countenance.
The man who had lost the letter and hadn't
reached the fan came up smiling, though red and
dishevelled, from underneath the seat, and started
to say something in an affable way, when his eye
fell on the paper, and then a look of horrified
astonishment came over him, and his hair—he
had only one—stood straight up. He uttered a
sort of paralysed whisper to the other man, and he
shrunlc-up on the spot, and his clothes seemed to
get too large for him, and his cuffs too long, and
the ends of his pants seemed to extend twelve
inches over his boots, and the tails of his coat
appeared to grow and trail on the floor, and his
countenance became so long that it extended
down into his lap. The second lady glanced at
them and grew alarmed, and then her eye
wandered to the letter .also, and a flush spread
over her face and neck and travelled right down
her back. And after that the four of them sat
erect and grim till the end of the act, and when it
was finished the man who had shrunk up so
suddenly made his escape, and didn’t come back,
and the second lady, and the gentleman with the
one hair, got up and made a sidelong shuffle as if
they wanted to do likewise. But the woman whb
held the letter fixed her eye upon them for just
one second, and they smiled idiotically and sat
down again. Then she also smiled, and getting
up moved slowly to the dressing-room and dis-
appeared, and the man she left behind mopped
his smoking head with his handkerchief and
seemed to wish he was dead. As for the man
who had escaped, he went down the stair so fast
that he seemed to whiz in the air with his own
velocity, and got into a cab ; and the next instant
the driver was whaling his horse like 40,000 cab-
men and travelling along the street faster than a
comet.

There is hope for the bald-headed man
yet. A Yankee surgeon asserts that by the skin-
grafting process a bald head can be covered by a
scalp teeming with healthy hair. We are pretty
bald ourselves and when skin-grafting becomes
cheap we mean to swap scalp-skins with a poodle
and set up in opposition to Parkes as a G.O.M.

The assets of a certain Northern bank are
said to consist of irreducible overdrafts, station
title deeds worth Jd. a pound, and bundles of
Mount Morgan scrip, on which £9 per share was
advanced during the boom.

# # *
The London correspondent of a Melbourne
daily describes how a young bridegroom of Vienna
went off with his bride in a furniture-van, in
which they intended to spend the honeymoon;
He (the correspondent) says he doesn’t think much
of the idea. We do. It is, at any rate, better
than allowing the time-payment bailiff to go off in
the furniture-van —with the furniture.
# # #
The Rev. T. Barningham, speaking at a
Methodist tea-meeting, held in Lilydale. Vic.,
lately, said that the presence of the Colonial Bank
manager had “given a tone to the meeting.” A
borrow-tone, perhaps. The money-changer is never
thrown out of the Temple now-a-days; he is
invited to step in and take a front seat.

That great and intelligent man, Major-
General Tulloch, states that “Sydney will shortly
be as safe as the guns mounted and forts can
make it.” Just so. Also, Major General Tulloch
will shortly be as tall as his height will allow, and
will have as much money belonging to him as the
coin in his possession will admit of. Great Scott!
Fancy paying an expensive Major-General to
supply such information as that.
# # #
A naval officer in Brisbane was seeing
someone off at Brisbane wharf. As the steamer
drew away, he took out his handkerchief to wave
farewell, and unintentionally drew out some-
thing else which fell with a splash into the
v ater. Now, it wasn’t what you think. It
was neither a prayer-book nor a pocket-pistol.
The officer himself couldn’t have told you what
had plumbed the river’s muddy depth—at lates,
not then. He returned to his hotel for breakfast,
and then, Oh ! then, where were his ** eating
teeth ” that cost him a score of sovereigns ? Ask
of the fishes that do haunt Brisbane’s wharves.
He had put in his “parade teeth,” and had care-
lessly placed the “eating ” set in a pocket, and —
but you all know, now.

FEDERATION OR NO FEDERATION..
The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal;
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pemell’s Meal..
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.
O Lord ! in whom I put my trust,
I merely wish to say
That Thou, who art so good and just,
Might’st take old P. away ;
Upon her knees Lucinda vows
She really wants a younger spouse.
Each day and night on Thee I call
For pity and relief—
The old ’un is no good at all,
He ought to come to grief.
Of that I’ve not the slightest doubt,
O ! Gracious Father, wipe him out.
I have repented of my sins,
For mercy now I beg :
Please give me strength for sticking pins
In my protector’s le.g
I’m weary of his wrinkled face —
The silent tomb’s his proper place.
When next I leave my little bed
To offer up a prayer,
I fain would find my husband dead
And free from earthly care.
Supply, O Lord, my daily needs,
And make me fit for widow’s weeds.

Theologians no longer speakwith authority.
They are content to suggest and to deprecate
hasty contradiction. Those who believed on
trust have passed into uncertainty. Those who
uphold orthodoxy cannot agree on what ground
to defend it. . . . Doctrines once fixed as a
rock are now fluid as water. — Fkoude.

The most remarkable painting in Paris
Salon this year is Berard’s “Jesus at the Phari-
see’s house,” of which an engraving is before us.
Jesus, painted in an unconventional and life-like
way, is the traditional figure, and to his feet
there crawls a magnificent Magdalen of the
Camille order, in modern evening dress, while the
guests are about 20 Parisian gentlemen of to-day,
mostly middle-aged, or old men, and most vividly
depicted, who wear expressions of sarcasm and
wonder.

(“ Tub Australian Star,” Sydney, June 2,1891.)
£lOO,OOO SWEEP.
(TO THE EDITOR OF “THE AUSTRALIAN STAR.”)
Sir,—Will you, through your widely-circulated
paper, inform me and the general public if it is a
fact that “ Tattersall George Adams ” has disposed
of the whole or part of his interest in the well,
known Tattersall’s sweep, and that it is now
carried on by a syndicate of Sydney people ? If a
syndicate have it in hand will it be conducted in
the fair and square manner that it was supposed
to be when George Adams had it himself.—Yours,
&0-. A SUBSCRIBER.
(“ The Australian Star,” Sydney, JuneS, 1891.)
“ TATTERSALL’S ” CONSULTATIONS,
In answer to “A Subscriber,” whose letter we
published in our issue of yesterday, we have to
Btate that upon inquiry we find there is no truth
whatever in the statement that “ Tattersall’s” con
sultations have been disposed of to a syndicate.
The consultations are still conducted (and
will continue to be as heretofore) under
the direction and sole proprietorship of
“ Tattersall,” and the same principle of
drawing will always be adhered to.

Brisbane Courier and Observer—the
morning and evening emanations from the
Puzacott family white elephant—have been re-
duced in size. Mute evidence that “law and
ordah ” has prevailed.
The Queensland Government are to raise
the wind by selling as much land as possible and
materially increasing the liquor-duties. The land-
grabber will profit by the first, and the hand-
workers will have to pay the larger part of the
second.
Agents required everywhere throughout
Australasia. Secretaries of friendly societies,
unions, &c., should write. Previous experience
unnecessary. Good pay and permanent positions.
For particulars, apply, the Co-Operative Irriga-
tion and Mercantile Society of Australia, Ltd.
4!) Queen-street, Melbourne.
The chairman of a western branch of the
N.S.W. Pastoralists’Union actually had the brass
to identify himself with the labour party during the
heat of the recent parliamentary election contest.
Political necessity is the mother of reversible
pants.
When a property changes hands in N.S.W.
the. auctioneer gets 2| per cent., the solicitor
about 1 per cent., and the Government is content
with a modest £ per cent, for stamp-duty on the
conveyance. Now, if the Government would take
say 5 or 10 per cent, stamp-duty on every trans-
fer, and 2h per cent, on every mortgage (it gets
nothing on mortgages at present), it would be
doing no injustice, and would help greatly to stop
land-booming. Labour party, please note.
A Socialism under ruling commercial conditions
—the Co-operative Irrigation Colonies.
Dear . Bulletin, —It is time we were done
with the squatter J.P. “Little Dick ”is a shep-
herd for a squatter, who is also a J.P. Little
Dick picked up a mate recently, and both got
drunk and were duly “run in.” The squatter
appeared on the bench. The charge was precisely
the same in each case, and both pleaded guilty.
Little Dick was fined 55., his mate was “ sent up ”
for a month. The squatter and his “hand” met
after the case, when the former said : “ Little
Dick, don’t you get drunk again. I could have
given you a month this morning, you know, like I
gave your mate.” “Yes, but you wanted me to
shepherd them rams.” “That’s so, Little Dick,
but don’t presume too often ; swagmen shepherds
are plentiful.” . . A squatter J.P. discharged
his servant without notice or payment. She sum-
moned him. There was no other J.P. around,
but that was a small matter for this sheep-magnate
—beg pardon, pastoralist. He mounted the
bench and heard the girl’s evidence; descended to
the witness-box as defendant, and gave his own
testimony to the grinning C.P.S., then re-mounted
the Bench and gave a verdict against the plaintiff,
Our informant forgot to tell us whether lie also
gave costs against the girl and pocketed the money.
Another squatter J.P. also had a difficulty with
his domestic—a new girl—who was rather fiery-
tempered. When he remonstrated with her, she
told him to “go further,” as she had discovered
that her “bed” consisted of a rotten mattress
which had been the camp-rags of a Chow. 'I he
squatter then said she might go, and that a cart
would be ready to take her back to “the town-
ship”—lB miles distant, on a lonely bush-track,
with scarcely a house on it, and a night coming on
as dark as pitch. The girl got ready, and the cart
duly arrived, driven by a bleary-eyedOhmair.an—•
presumably the one who had roosted on the
mattress. “I’m not going with that dirty ould
haythen,” said Mary. “Then you can go and
sleep under a tree,” said squatter J.P. Fancy
this to a white girl! There is a well-
known temperance-lecturer who somehow in-
curred the resentment of an equally well-known
bush-magistrate. TheT.L. started his open-air
show one day as usual. The magistrate called to
a rough, strong man, a brewer’s employb, and
said—“ I’ll give you a crown to go up and smack
Mr. (the T.L.) in the face.” “No fear;
because if I do I’ll get into trouble.” “If you
get into trouble,” said the beak, “ I’ll bail you
out and I’ll be on the Bench in the morning and
see that the case is dismissed as a trivial one.”—
Yours, Bushman.
The London & Sydney Tea Co. supply squatters
and farmers at wholesale prices.

"N THE SUPREME COURT OF QUEENS-
LAND.
MATRIMONIAL CAUSES JURISDICTION.
The Chief Justice No. of 1891.
BETWEEN HELEN STEWART MULHOL-
LAND, PETITIONER, and FREDERICK
ALFRED MULHOLLAND. RESPON-
DENT.
VICTORIA, by the Grace of God of the
(1.5.) United Kingdom of Great Britain and
Ireland, Queen, Defender of the Faith.
TO FREDERICK ALFRED MULHOLLAND,
of Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales,
Commercial Traveller.
WE Command you that within One Calendar
Month after service of this on you, inclusive of the
day of such service, you do cause an appearance to
be entered for you in Our Supreme Court of
Queensland, then and there to make answer to a
Petition wherein HELEN STEWART MUL-
HOLLAND, claiming to have been married to
you, is the Petitioner, and a copy whereof, sealed
with the seal of our said Court, is herewith served
upon you. AND TAKE NOTICE that in default
of your so doing the hearing of the charges thereof
proved in due course of law and the pronouncing
of sentence therein will be proceeded with in your
absence notwithstanding.
WITNESS, The Honorable Sir Charles
Lilley, Knight, the Chief Justice of
Queensland, this thirteenth day of April,
in the fifty-fourth year of our Reign, and
in the year of Our Lord One Thousand
Eight Hundred and Ninety-one.
(1.5.) ALFRED DOWN.
Deputy-Registrar.
IN THE SUPREME COURT OF QUEENS-
LAND.
MATRIMONIAL CAUSES JURISDICTION.
In the matter of the petition of HELEN
STEWART MULIIOLLAND, of Mary-
(l*s.) borough, in the Colony of Queensland,
praying Divorce against her husband,
FREDERICK ALFRED MULHOL-
LAND.
WEDNESDAY, THE SEVENTEENTH DAY
OF JUNE, 1891.
UPON HEARING the Solicitors for the
Petitioner, and upon reading the Affidavits of
THOMAS O’SULLIVAN and HELEN
STEWART MULHOLLAND filed the seven-
teenth day of June, 1891.
IT IS ORDERED that service of a sealed
copy of the Citation and Petition in this matter
by delivering the same to Mrs. Ernest, of George-
street, Bundaberg, the mother of the Respondent,
at Bundaberg, personally, and by sending a
sealed copy of the said Citation and Petition by a
sealed prepaid registered post letter addressed to
the Respondent in care of T. F. Mulholland,
Her Majesty’s Customs, Sydney, and by adver-
tising a copy of this order and of the said Citation
in newspapers published in Sydney, Brisbane,
Maryborough, Bundaberg, and Townsville, and
in newspapers published at or nearest to Cunna-
mulla, Blackall, Winton, and Hughenden, each of
such advertisements to be inserted twice at
intervals of one week shall be good and sufficient
service of the Citation and Petition herein.
Dated the Nineteenth Day of June, 1891.
PATRICK REAL, J.
M. J. O’SuijLiVAN, (1.5.)

are so cheap that anybody can afford to own

bill.” Therefore, because cheap, do not think
they are only toys or “ good enough for children.”
They have all the traits of the best makers, and as
they add to these unusual strength and durability,
besides cheapness, they are essentially the watches
for the mechanic, the farmer, the clerk, the work-
ing man and woman—in a word, The Watches
for the People.

WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
Ist, It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely

agonies.

face, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently; seareh-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
6th. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.

standing reward has been offered to any one who

authenticity, tinder these circumstances is it to

do well to observe that they get the genuine article
and not one of the many spurious preparations
made up to trade upon the enormous reputation
which St. Jacobs Oil has built up for itself ujon
the solid foundation of merit.

iind should i cease to live in imperial; riot and

of this wild and costly grub they are to eat brown

orders,” who, when they peel a potato, rise up j
recklessly and throw away the skin. And in one j
sense this elevated she-prig is right, for the ‘‘lower
orders ” are in a tight place, and must give up I
something, if they want to avoid insolvency, j
Most probably they will give up the old nobility
as an act of unavoidable self-denial, and mortify j
the flesh by stooping the interest on the National !

, t thm flu in a Melbourne i paper : “When
first discovered the body was floating face-down-
wards, and the scalp was flopping about on the
surface with the motion of the water.” That is
the principal feature of the story, and there is
about a column of it, accompanied by tears and
poetry. The substance of it is about this : One
female dressed in black, and furnished with a
baby, probably afflicted with blind staggers ; one
large eel in pocket and crab fixed in back-hair;
pawn-ticket and one pint of slush in another
pocket. First seen by Constable O’Hooligan ;
fished out by Constable O’Higgins ; carried to
the morgue with the assistance of Constable
O’Finnigan. Statement at the inquest by
Constable O’Hogan that he believed the deceased
was identical with a person that he had frequently
obser-r-rved at different times in various places at
all hours of the day, but hadn’t spoken to her,
owing to her being appar-r-rently just the same
as any other per-r-rson. Further evidence by a j
Salvation Army official to the effect that he
didn’t know the deceased either, but if he had

of funds, in consequence of which donations are

One of the curiosities of Melbourne is an
insolvent money-lender. Less than 12 months ago
his advertisement glared out conspicuously in all
the papers, stating that he was prepared to lend
any sum from £5 to £5OOO without security,
promptly, with the utmost secresy, and at lowest
current rate, repayment any time you liked.
Also he would buy up your interest under your
will; or purchase your of adiahee
jnoney on a bill with two good names on the back,
or take over your reversionary interest, or be a
father to you without any extra charge, or if
money had been left to, you he would fix up an
arrangement whereby you could have a sudden
and immediate burst, and then the money would
be left to him instead. Then his advertisement
disappeared, and he took to leaning up against
lamp-posts in a ragged and pensive state, and lent
£5 to £5OOO rio longer. It is rumoured that he got
hard-up through some temporary extravagance,
and borrowed money from himself on a bill.
Then he couldn’t pay when it fell due, and he
called at his own office and renewed the document
at a steep percentage, and when the second bill
becaiiie pavable there was a painful scene between
him and himself, and he had to give a mortgage
bn all his property, and the interest fell into
arrear, and there were more bills, and in a
moment of indignation he callecf himself a skin-
flint and a money-grabber, and then he rushed at
himself and threw himself out of the office, and he
sued himself for libel and had to pay damages,
and after that he brought a counter charge of
assault against himself, and finally he sold him-
self up, and the proceeds went in law expenses.
And now his premises are closed, and the man
who wants to blow out his annuity knocks in vain
at the door, and arouses only a hollow echo, like
the ghost of a lost mortgage; and the elderly
maiden lady who sleeps on the floor because her
furniture has all been seized under a bill of sale,
comes to weep at the keyhole and call down
judgments on his head no longer ; and even the
elderly man with the bald head, who rashly went
“shecurity” for a friend, has abandoned the
place, and all is emptiness.

Messrs. Perkins and Co., Limited.
CITY BREWERY, BRISBANE.
One of the best known brewing firms in Australia
is Messrs. Perkins & Co., Limited, having an
establishment in Brisbane and another at Too-
woomba, The headquarters of this important
Company is the City Brewery, Brisbane—with
! premises extending from Mary-street to Margatef-
street with two frontages of 198 feet each.
Although the details of brewing as practised bjy
Messrs. Perkins & Co., Limited-, is much in keep-

breweries, yet the generalPrganisaViori’, 'cleanliness
and effectiveness 'of. their establishment, is worth

Queensland. Messrs. Perkins & Co., having

A cu.ri.ous red fish, the alulua, turns up in
shoals about the Hawaiian Islands at irregular
intervals, and when they invade the harbour of
Honolulu the natives recognise that bne bf the
royal family is doomed. A feW mofiths ftgo this
visitation came along, ftnd the inhabitants resolved
that Slug Kalakajia Was about to light out for a
bettet lpud. { And, sure enough, Kalakaua did
light out just 20 days later. It is a very poor
royal family that hasn’t some solemn portent
deposited in a cool spot, to trot out when a death
is going to happen. Even the Imperial House of
Britain is provided in this respect, i When the
Queen begins to scatter money about recklessly,
and forgets about Brown, it will be a sure sign
that something is looming.

RHEUMATISM versus MICROBE
KILLER.
294 Castleresgh-street, June 25, 1891.
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co., Gentlemen,—For
forty years I have been a sufferer from rheu-
matism, and latterly in the most aggravated f5rm,
often having acute attacks which made me almost
helpless for days, insomuch that it was with the
greatest pain and difficulty that I could move
about at all, and only able to get up stairs on my
hands and knees. A friend who had tried the
“Microbe Killer” with beneficial results sent me
a jar of the medicine, which I took, and I have
now taken three jars of “Microbe Killer” and
the result has been a great wonder to myself and
my friends, for now I can get about freely, with-
out pain. I go up and down stairs without any
trouble, and I am greatly improved in health
every way. In fact my suffering from rheumatism
seems a thing of the past, and I can recommend
the “ Microbe Killer ” to anyone with confidence,
knowing what it has done for me. Several have
tried your medicine on my recommendation for
other diseases —and one a cancer patient—with
the same beneficial results. —Yours respectfully,
MRS. WHITAKER,
