[?]
846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, July 1, 1891
My DKAR MOORABINDAt
1t is to be sincerely hoped, now that more women are,
from inclination or necessity, going in for the single life,
that the path of the lonely spinster-will be a little more
rosy than it hitherto has been, more especially in connec-
tion with boarding-houses. Only recently an American
actress was refused admission to an Australian hotel
simply because she hadn’t an alleged husband among her
traps ; and the hard fight any woman has to get a den at
a boarding-house is enough to drive her to the altar. I
was parleying with a hash-house duchess on the subject
of women boarders the other day. “ Why don’t you like
them as well as men?” quoth I—“ don’t they keep earlier
hours and eat less ?” But the high prophetess of rechauffe
slowly shook her head. “Lady boarders, indeed!” she
said, “I wouldn’t take one for 10 guineas a week. It’s
hot water if the weather’s a bit dull; then she knocks
me up at 2 a.m. because the cat ran up the stairs and she
thought it was a burglar; then complains regularly that
the sheets aren’t aired ; next she wants a housemaid dis-
missed because she's lost an inch of faded ribbon ; she
flirts at and ogles my best boarder to spooning-point, and
then I am solemnly called upon to dismiss him because he
tried to kiss her on the stairs ! No more lady boarders
for me,” concluded she, warmly ; “ I have tried them,
and now this establishment is for men only.”' But I
couldn’t help smiling a cold and frosty smile as I drifted
away down the street. You see, this lady had four mar-
riageable daughters and but marriageable daughters
are not the only reason why the unattached damsel has a
difficulty in getting a roof over her devoted head—espe-
cially when she trickles round without a persona l
reference.
Apropos spinsters, it ought to be a warning to the
frantic supporters of the W omen’s College, that of five ladies
who tore through an English ’varsity last year and came
out with alphabetical tails to their names and other
honours—only one has achieved a husband, while the
others are holding mediocre teaching appointments,
here, there and everywhere. Now, I wonder if the local
girl grad, really grasps the fate she is preparing for her-
self by “ cramming ” during her attractive days ? The
eternal grind of underpaid teaching—the mob of stupid,
rebellious pupils are all she has to hope for ? “ What,"
says somebody, “is to prevent her marrying?” Why,
this. Bound down to her books when she is at her
prettiest and best, she emerges-a B. A. perhaps—at the
age, say, of twenty-two, with the face of a woman of
thirty, and the stooping shoulders of a pedagogue. The
average man doesn’t want to marry an encyclopaedia, and
the woman who has spent her beauU du diable in acquir.
ing a vast amount of college information is passed over in
favour of some fresh-faced little fool who opens two wide
astonished eyes when George loftily tells her that gas is
extracted from coal. As a leading Sydney doctor—con-
nected “at home” with a family of rapid female pedants
—laughed to me recently, “ Sophie is a 8.A., poor girl.
A Sydney friend went to England the other day and they
asked him to stop with them, but he was bent on holiday-
making, couldn’t stand the intellectual pressure and
fled. Ths worst of it is that all the young men seem to
have fled, too.”
Come out—as they allege—principally to do the sun-
trick in balmy Australia, Sir Charles and Lady Hall 6
were more than disgusted at the muddy, squally weather
which signalised their reception at the MStropole on 24th
inst. Nevertheless there were mobs at it, and the lift for
some time was full of dazed visitors who hadn’t the
foggiest idea which way to go when they got to the second
(Stage and looked round in a helpless way until a waiter
magnanimously put them through. Lady Hall A-som-
ewhat stouter than last year (she enjoyed the voyage out
immensely)—wore a festive reception array in true pinky
heliotrope; the corselet bodice of velvet and the skirt of
silk ; full sleeves of matching chiffon trimmed with bands
of silver-spangled galon which also trimmed the bodio-
finished off at the neck with Directoire flounce i.
embroidered chiffon; bonnet in basket-straw trimmed
}n flowers and bows and strings of dark-purple velvet,
diamond brooch. Mddle. Filliinger (a small, smooth-
haired little being) in claret-surah with “dove’’-front in
cream, with gloves; Mrs. Charles Hall 4, electric-blue
cloth, bonnet in cream, white gloves; MadameKellermann
in a fetching visite of black velvet, jet embroidery and
fox-fur, small black jet bonnet. Mrs. Vanderveer Greene
in a workmanlike gown of bottle-green cloth and beaver-
fur. Bessie Rignold, in a dove-coloured cloak, came with
William Rignold, who has oaken to waxing his mustivy
since the trip ’Ome ; and Captain Le Patourel was seen
for once without that funny little dog at his heels. After-
noon tea was served here, there and everywhere, and a
crackling fire made the crowded reception-room a little
too cosy, perhaps.
It is clever of Lady Hall 6 to gown herself so charmingly
in public, for though the soulless portion of the female
audience may allow its attention to wander from even the
most seraphio fiddling, yet it never unglues its eye from a
fetching turn-out, and so listens in spite of itself. At the
first Hall 6 concert in the Centennial Hall on a recent wet
night, Lady H. wore a beautiful biscuit-coloured satin,
brocaded in bouquets of pale-green flowers and trimmed
with a basque of pleated pale-green ohiffon, which also
fell from the front of the diamond-studded corsage;
diamonds were also worn in her neatly-arranged hair. La
Filliinger (who had not quite gauged the hall and was
inaudible when singing with the half-voice) looked the
very double of a Sydney Judge’s daughter—fit pf her
gown, way of standing and all; and Burton, in black and
red, quite spoilt herself by that big bottle-brushy aigrette.
In the audience—a large proportion of which was bare-
necked sassiety girl—l only saw two thrills worth alluding
to. One, worn by a bride (mind you, I really didn’t know
■he wassich—l only guessed so through the trousseau-
like newness of her gown and wrap and the confiding
angle she described towards the sloping shoulder of her
attendant swain) was of sea-green bengaline trimmed
with gold galon jewelled in emeralds, the gown finished
off with green chiffon and ribbons ; the other, worn by a
blonde society miss, was of black silk, the bodice cut low
and filled in with drawn black gauze, and trimmed with
flashing emerald passementerie, crimson velvet wrap.
The second Halle concert, on 27th ult., was a largely-
attended function, seeing as how it was “V.R.,”anda
nice, starry evening too. Lord and Lady Jersey, the
eldest daughter, Captain “Chumlcy” and Mr. Goschen,
were posed on the red fauteils of patronage, half-a-yard
off the stage on the ground-floor, while the reserved
balconyites, who expected the gubernatorial party up
there as usual, gnashed their teeth and focussed their
opera-glasses on the distinguished bevy. There wasn’t
much worth focussing, though. Lady Jersey wore a
trying black silk dinner-gown, made with a bertha bodice
and no sleeves, relieved with a cluster of pinky heliotrope
flowers on the left shoulder, and a three-tier ruby and
diamond necklace ; wrap, in scarlet, lined with white and
trimmed gold bullion tassels; managerial nosegay in
pink, purple and white flowers ; her daughter, in white
and cream, trimmed with silver braid, and white cloak
edged with swansdown. On the stage—need I say it ?
Lady Halil had the noblest frocking of them all (the
badly-dressed audience—lots in hats and jac.Kets, lots in
pointless nun’s veiling rags—looked very dismal as she
came forth in a buttercup satin, brocaded with a line
design in white flag lilies and leaves, finished at the hem
with a flounce of yellow chiffon, caught with curves of
silk guipure, the bodice trimmed with guipure, ending
in gold fringe and finished with elbow sleeves and deep
ruche of yellow chiffon; diamond ornaments.) Lady H-
was in inspiring form, and the “adagio” movement of
the Beethoven (duet)Sonata in E Flat, withits artistically:
played organ chords made lots of people think the loca 1
white elephant was being introduced in a sub-rosa sort of
way.
The musical programme was all pure joy, needless to
say, but perhaps a little over the head of a typical fat
lady in an embroidered opera-cloak in front of me, who
took up her programme with a resigned sigh and com-
menced reading the words of the songs when Sir Charles
Halil sat down. By the way, why doesn’t the “Much
Travelled,” junior, see that his minions enforce the rule
keeping people out during the performance of a number ?
When Sir Charles finished the allegro in Waldstein’s
sonata and paused between the movements, a regular
tribe of late-comers came crashing through the doorways,
and the artist had to wait quite five minutes ere they
settled down. Which plays the very mischief with
continuity, and brings one back to earth with a bump, a s
it were.
The last June “ at home ” of Government House on 23rd
ultimo, was really a concert with refreshments com-
mencing at the blinking hour of a quarter to ten when
the last guest arrived and Lady Jersey was at liberty to
leave her position as welcoming hostess and come into the
ball-room (where the concert was held) to set the pro-
gramme going. The card—sweetly-scented and printed
in blue—was attractively compiled. Mrs. Vanderveef
Greene (who wore a handsome gown of white satin draped
with pale-bluecrlpe chiffon with a skirt panelofcutsteeland
jewels, the bodice edged with pale-blue ostrich tips) exer-
cised her liquid expressional contralto in two soli and a
duet—her first item being “ Three Fishers.” Eille Nor-
wood whistled ; Ernest Hutcheson (a society girl who was
dying to hear herself talk, began to chat during his
“ Rhapsody ” but was promptly hushed down) gave two
items; and several well-known amateurs contributed.
As for the frocking ’twas a fine evening and fine feathers
resulted. Lady Jersey wore cream satin brocaded in tiny
floral designs and trimmed with little tufts of dark-green
feathers, green feather fan, and pearl and diamond
jewellery; Miss Stephen wore a Jubilee-red gown ; a
doctor’s wife in grey had too much drapery on her pin
cushioned figure; and a vice-consul’s lady in white
armure and chiffon frills looked very angelic. “God Save
the Queen ” told the guests when it was time to go.
Referring to the Jerseys—that little habit they’ve gcj,
of remaining while the final “God Save,” &c., is being
sawn out merely as hint to the audience to go away, i g
rather unwise, and tends to make the vice-regal depar-
ture a lame and impotent spectacle. At the Halil
concert they remained standing while Mr. Sykes pounded
out the Anathema, and then cut their gubernatorial stick
amidst profound and surprised silence and the mental
execrations of those “loyal” wights who don’t like to
leave before the Guv’nah. Now, it is more imposing and
effective (vice-regal people should never lose sight of the
circus element in their goings and comings) for the
Queen’s representative to stalk out as the moth-eaten
National Anathema is being played. For myself, I (though,
being a woman, I doat on Royalty, efcc.) really hate the
air of “God Save the Queen.” It is so odiously Little-
Bethel-hymn-like. What a wretched, hypocritical, psalmy
production it sounds as compared with the “Marseillaise,’’
or the Russian hymn, or “The Watch on the Rhine”—or,
for the matter of that, the song which the chief blackfellow
chants at the corroboree 1
Quite the fashionable caper during the late wet weather
has been to “do ” the new and palatial seven-storied
Australia Hotel, situated in Castlereagh-street. From
the vast Moorish vestibule with the crimson and fawn
marble stairway leading out of it, also, on the other side,
the large bar-room, the many mirrors which seem to
point the moral that the attendant Hebes’ charms are
going to stand reflecting, right up to the flag-pole on the
“Excelsior” dome, the Australia is perfectly planned
and appointed. But unless one “inventoried” like an
an auctioneer, one couldn’t give an idea of the general
artistic sumptuousness of this splendid hotel. The Aus-
tralia certainly has “success” written all over its gilded
brow. Fancy, oh women ! the rapture of camping in a
palace where the management sets apart a private room
for ladies who are too lazy (the courteous, violet-eyed
manager said “indisposed”) to rise and dress for the
general breakfast. Though all this joy isn’t publicly
available as yet, several of the suites are already engaged.
You say I looked pained and tired—that mournfully I
demean—that gone is the smile you admired while my
laugh sounds as if it were hired—well, I’ve been to
“Evangeline.” Why the man who knocked out the
dialogue, and the other being who owns up to having
concocted the music, allow their names to be blazoned on
the bill isn’t exactly apparent, to we—it nly shows,
though, how utterly incompetent anybody who translates
his inner consciousness into quavers and commas is to
truly estimate the value (or otherwise—principally other-
wise) of his own work. In “ Evangeline ” the music was
Chinese-y, v ith lots of big drum' in it- and the talk, 1
think, would disgrace a biscuit-bag. But the dresses were
brilliant, and the good, old Gaiety principle of tights,
followed out to the last possibility, helped to keep the
stalls full. Fanny Liddiard’s white satin trimmed with
bands of red velvet and silk pom-poms in the two colours
was “ gyp,” as also was her yellow silk classic garment
with yellow chiffon sleeves and a girdle of mock jewels.
The bead-clad Amazons were a sparkling spectacle, too r
but intellect demands something more in an alleged
“burlesque ” than silk and sawdust. “ Evangeline ’’ is a,
spectacle for children—very credulous children.
One or two hostesses who have introduced the paid
professional element into their musical shivoos are
mourning over the extreme “ difficulty ” of these hired
warblers who chortle their little lilt, and then, unless the
distracted hostess comes personally each time to over-
whelm them with flattery and keep them amused until
the next item, sit with their noses in the air smiling a
superior, tooth-achey smile at the unoffending crowd. The
other evening a well-known Sydney singer snapped out
her vocal contribution and then announced her intention
of going home as the hostess did not come to thank her,
and stood with her back turned. But her amour propre
was soothed somehow, and she stayed to supper. Then a
Sydney hostess, who would fain encourage local music,
wrote to a not very special songstress offering her five
guineas to come and sing two songs. But that proud and
expensive being replied that if Mrs. Dash would kindly
include her cab-fare, Miss Blank would accept her terms.
Needless to say another and less extortionate nightingale
got the contract.
The second Sydney Rl-union, held at Nithsdale the
other evening, was a most successful dance, brightened by
lots of pretty frocks. Miss Blanche Turner wore a lemon-
coloured brocade trimmed with flounces and basques of
lemon-coloured chiffon, the skirt trimmed with tufts of
ostrich feathers; Miss Annie Wilson, in well-cut black and
jet, white feather fan ; another pretty frock was of pink
silk and chiffon, floral fan. But—there is always a “but”
—one strange get-up reminded me of the old lady who
didn’t like glaring colours, but preferred blue and red —
fancy a ti'ain half blue and half crimson ! Also, one or
two eccentric damsels in night-gown costumes had their
bushy hair arranged a la darkest Africa.
There is a biting of thumbs in certain quarters over the
alteration of the location of a charming actress, and some
people say the first house would suit the exacting lady
better than the final fixture. An alleged reason given for
the change is that there isn’t room for her scenery in
theatre No. 1, and that the dressing-rooms are too
“ornery” for the beautiful being, but, as a matter of
cold, emotionless fact, all the drops and props and clothes
of her most dazzling production, the only accessories she
brought with her (the rest being manufactured here) are
at present stored in theatre No. I—proving the existence
of adequate storing space ; and, as for the dressing-rooms
—the lessee of No. 1 intended fitting up the big property-
room for her to pin her clothes on in, and if that didn’t
suit her, what would? But they say the theatrical milk
in the cocoanut is easily accounted for. Somebody wants
to boom the Pitt-street show-trade for certain transparent
reasons.
Speaking of actress's “ props” reminds me that one or
two people are railing because the Divine Sara has
brought several cases of preserved vegetation “ just as
if,” they chorus indignantly, “ there wasn’t any greenery
in Australia !” This is the sort of person who couldn’t
detect the slightest incongruity if Cleopatra’s barge hove
in sight festively adorned with bottle-brushes and
gum-leaves, or if her gilded palace-garden was simply
a maze of “ tickey ” bush. The divine and clear-sighted
Sara would have a long way to look before she found a
passable substitute for Nile cactus or the weird Nile
reeds in the Sunny South—unless she resorted to
diagrams, green paint and calico.
From England the news comes that on 4th April, at St.
Michael’s Church, London, Miss Nellie Cox, of Sydney,
became the bride of the blonde and silent Captain W. M.
Le Poer Trench—eldest son of Colonel Trench—who is
well remembered in Sydney as the reticent A.D.C. to
Lord Carington. The wedding was very quiet. There
w'ere no bridesmaids—and the pretty bride was married
in a tailor-built gown.
Another wedding (on 23rd ult.) was a very glittering
pageant—Miss Bessie Aarons—youngest daughter of Mr.
Louis Aarons (of the Exchange) was united to Mr. Walter
Hardy, son of Mr. John Hardy, of Sydney, in a gown of
satin duchesse, chiffon, point lace and orange-blossoms—
her five bridesmaids in cream cloth costumes trimmed
with bullion lace and emerald passementerie. The cere-
mony took place at “The Pines,” Randwiek, and the
wedding-party was numerous and handsomely attired.
Ere setting out for the honeymoon the happy pair joined
in a quadrille with the bridesmaids and groomsmen.
An ideally quiet wedding was that of Miss Christina
M’lntosh, daughter of Hon. John M’lntosh, of “ Lind-
say,” Darling Point, to Mr. Harry M. Flood (son of
Mr. E. Flood) on 17th ult. The wedding took place quite
privately in the drawing-room at “Lindsay,” only rela-
tives and one or two very old friends being present. The
bride wore a travelling dress of crimson serge with velvet
plateau hat en suite trimmed with jet; her one bridesmaid
in cream. Honeymoon at Laurel Park, Burradoo, the

On June 17th Mr. “Jack” Woolcock, barrister, of
Brisbane, was married to Miss Gertrude Harpur, of Sum*
merhill, Sydney. The bride (who followed her 15 brides-
maids instead of them following her) wore white
Bengaline and brocade, and left for the Bowral honeymoon

suite. Of the bridesmaids, 12 wore nasturtium-red gowns
trimmed with beaver-fur, the three little maidens wearing

wedding-party.
It is related of Sara B. that on the evening she stayed at
the Grosvenor, Sydney, dinner was served, gongs clanged,
and chairs had been arranged, and the company seated—
but no Sara came. High and low, no Sara either, until*
when everybody was at the last extremity of bewilder-
ment, a silvery laugh was heard, and out popped La
Bernhardt from under the table! She said she wanted to
study how people put their feet in sitting down.

Quong Tart gave his annual social and hall to his em.
ployees at his King-street premises on Wednesday last,
week, when each had the right to introduce a
visitor, so the girls brought along their best boys, the
boys their best girls, and the aggregate totted np to over
150 souls, who ate and drank and danced and sung till
four in the morning. During the evening Mr. Tart, who
was in great voice, announced that he had been waited on
by five delegates from the Trades and Labour Council,
who asked him to induce his employees to join the
Waitresses’ Union, and said that he left them to choose
for themselves. If they desired to become members of
the union, they were left free to do so. If they did not
desire to join, then he would be be unwilling to persuade
them to take that step.
Sir George Macleay, who accompanied
Captain Sturt in his expedition of 1830 clown the
Murrumbidgee and Murray, has just died in
England aged 82. At 80 he married a young
wife.

how the wind is blowing, a weathercock be placed

What is the matter with (shall we say “ ex.,” for

With the first successes of the N.S.W.
Labour party, Sydney Echo became absolutely
frenzied with impotent, rage, but as the political
Labour barometer ascends, it-gradually, calms
down. Next week it will probably begin to pat
the Labour party on the back. This journalistic
cooling-down recalls the high-toned French paper
on Napoleon’s escape from Elba : “The tiger has
escaped.” Next day : “The usurper has landed.”
Then, “Napoleon is advancing.” “Bonaparte is
well on his way.” “The Emperor is at the
Tuileries —Virc L’Empercur /” Yah !
Every man who was made a special mark
for the vengeance cf constituted authority or
monopoly during the recent strike labour troubles
has been avenged by the N.S.W. voters. Harry
Hoyle, dismissed by the N.S.W. Railway Com-
missioners, was elected at the top of the poll for
Redfern—and Schey was close up. W. A. Murphy,
Secretary and Organiser of the Marine Officers’
Association, who was the target of unlimited
abuse during the strike, was triumphantly re-
turned for Balmain. Arthur Rae, of the N.S.W.
Shearers’ Union, sentenced to two or three years’
goal for having incited labourers to break their
agreements (only one such act on his part was be-
fore the court, mind you, but he was committed
to prison for a separate term on account of each
man he influenced) got in triumphantly for the
Murrumbidgee.

Teles of the Old Regime.
No. IX.—The Liberation of the Fir At Three.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)

The words, of the call beat against the walls Of
the shaft, were thrust (so „it seemed) from side to
side, aad throbbed into silence in the darkness at
its bottom. There was no echo, but up the sixty
feet of timbered opening rung instantly metallic
sounds, and then, a moment later, ari, answering
shout. •
“ Yes, sir ! Here, sir 1”
“Show yourselves!”
The speaker bent over the platform and peered
down.
“One, two —three, four —five, six !” he counted.
“Right! . Auyr complaints ?”
“ No, sir !” came up*,
“Good night l And, Page, the grating ?”
Lieut, Qooke, iu command of the Port Arthur
Coal Mlfees 3>ep6t, with its gang of to-be-pecu-
ii»*ly-punished convicts, so ordered; and Lieut.
CoofcftV myrmiclons in present attendance thrust
upon the mouth of the pit ' the iron grating
Which saved His Majesty the cost of a night
sentry’s services by blocking the egress of the
prisoners in No. 3 pit. The men of the Regime
trusted few things to chance, The half-doßeh
men in the galleries were ddubl.y-irottea—trebly,
if you reckon also the links Which bound each to
another ; they were separated from the surface by
sixty feet of ladderless shaft; arid yet had there
been nothing else to hold them to the spot Where
the tender mercies of the Local Government had
lodged them, the men of the Regime .thought the
chances were still in favour of their escaping.
Whereupon the Regime, being crippled in the
strength of its military guard, hit upon the ex-
pedient of a grating to take the place of a sentry.
A grating with double padlocks, with inch-thick
bars forged in the workshops at the Settlement
specially under the eye of Commandant Dr.
Rossell, and with interstice's, perhaps, of twq.
fingers’ breadth. If the pit-workers had any chance
of escape that grating removed it. So the Regime
thought.
* # #
Yet so utterly oblivious were those wretches
immured in the galleries of No. 3, “ Murderers’
Pit”—No. 1 was “Forgers’,” and No. 2 was
“ Gomorrah ”—of the views and desires of the
Authorities that for some weeks past they had been
bent upon -escaping. They didn’t care a “ brass ’’
for the Regime, or for those illustrations'of the.
Regime's loving-kindness—the irons, the ladder-
less state of the shaft and the grating. Their
difficulty was—food. It was useless attempting
to escape without a stock of food, sufficient to last
them for three weeks er a month. And the
supreme problem of existence .to them resolved
itself into the question how they were to steal the
tations.
There were six of them, only a moiety of them
murderers. And those who had not murdered
were particularly unwilling to face the grim
recesses of the scrub which surrounded the “ coal-
mines ” without an ample supply of rations.
Macquarie Harbour had been broken up a year or
so, aud the murderous moiety were part of the lot
from Hell’s Gates. That was why the other three
were especially disinclined to attempt a bolt with-
out well-filled pockets. Each of the Harbour men
was doing a second “ life ” term for escaping from
a Sarah island gang. Each had bolted, and each
had been recaptured—with a slavering mouth, that
symptom of an infamy. The non-murderous Three
paled, in the gloom of the galleries, when they
learnt, each from his brother-hewer, what had
caused the slavering. The gourmand is ever
smacking his lips over the remembrance ot -some
especially choice menu. The Three with the
blood dyed records were gourmands.
* * *
Jones, Tooth, Hardy—these constituted
the murderful moiety. Phillips, Bunt, West—
these were the three who paled at the recital
of the others’ banquet. By chains of 3fi links
(say nine feet from the basil of one. waist to the
Waist of the other), was Jones fastened to Phillips,
and Tooth to Bunt, and Hardy to West. Until
Death or his twin-brethren of the Regime chose to
part them were the couples to remain thus em-
braced.
In this companionship the men walked and
slept—“mealed’’and “reported” themselves. In
this companionship were they to live and die.
That was the plain English of the arrangement—
of the latest-invented detail of Colonel George
Arthur’s newly-established Penal Settlement.
• * •
At the mines the denizens of “ Murderers’!’
Were known as “The Twins.” Each “twin”
wore a pair of trousers, a basil, or iron waist-
band, and his double ankle-shackles. Once a
month, for two hours, he wore also a shirt, boots
and a cap. It was on Inspection Sunday, when
Dr. Rossell came down to the Mines to “report
on the well-being of the transports and other con-
victs,” that this additional clothing was issued.
One other daily prerogative was to every man the
half of the nine-foot chain before mentioned.
Their heavy andairy costume offeteMHttle or no'
impediment to the workers turning out daily
(Sundays excepted) their allotted tasks. The
hewing of 15 hundredweight, such “hundred”
consisting of 1201bs. —not more than three out of
the fifteen were to be composed of slack and small
coal—made up, with the haulage to the shaft, the
cutting of galleries and the picking out of pillars,
the daily quota of each man. Not very difficult
work, if it had not been for the circumstances
that only for two hours per month did the ganger
draw a breath of air which was uncontaminated
with coal-dust, and that the daily ration-scale
permitted him only l(>oz. uncooked maize-meal,
12oz. of meat, with bone, loz. of salt, |oz. of soap,
and unlimited prater. But these circumstances
were not. calculated to strengthen the physical
stamina, and even with the aid of the allowance
of candles (four per man tier week, which were as
often as not treated as adjuncts to the larder) the
task sometimes proved beyond their strength to
perform.
With the reminiscence of the Harbour banquets
constantly before them, the Three -with the
Stained Records grew hungry and went weaker to
their work, and failed to perform it, and, to save
themselves from being flogged on Inspection,
Sundays, confiscated pretty frequently the fruits
of their chain-mates’ toil.
Phillips was lugging his basket one evening to
the end of the gallery to have it hoisted to the
pit-mouth, when Jones, necessarily going with him
because of their mutual chain, insinuated that he
would be obliged by Phillips handing him lialf-a-
-’ basketful. ....
“ Only ’arf, Ben ! I*ll giv’ it yer back sum’
time.” ■• ■■■■
“Nd!” said Phillips, resolutely. “You .owe
me two baskets now. And being short last
inspection got me a dozen.”
“Yer won’t, won’t yer! Yer*wott’t give me
.’arf?”
; “No!” •
“Then I’ll take all 1”
And, being a man of his word, he-did. In Hie
Overman Page’s tally-book that night Jones was
credited with two baskets;' Phillips witfi,two-
thirds of one—and that “small.’*;- *
“Look ’ere, No. 4”—Phillips was “4”—
shouted the.overman. “ Short agen ! Lor’, yer’ll
’av ei? devil of er lot ter make up by Stmday week.
An’ if yer don’t it’ll be two dozen riext time,
mark my words! Com’dant won’t stand no
nonsense !”
The process of confiscation went on pretty fre-
quently—and almost regularly, arid regularity
also was conspicuous in the matter of the floggings
received by Phillips, Bunt, and West. The Lieu-
tenant and the Overman wondered how it was
that the three desperate Harbour men
to produce thhif tally of tootle, while the others of
better reputation were nearly always “short.”
For,' of course, the’story of the confiscations could
not be told. The Three would have made dread-
fully short work of the man who informed against
their tyranny. ‘
' It was the victims of the confiscation policy who
-first pronounced for a “ bolt.” They had got tired
of the. treatment to which they were subjected by
their •chain-mates arid, the authorities, combined ;
and, as the period of their confinement lengthened,-
their bodily weakness would _increase, though the
hiboftr'and the pu ishirients would grow heavier.
If they could only escape !
-* * #
One day, Phillips was “outsider ” oil his
chain. That is to say 7, he was working nearest the
shaft iu a gallery.... His ehain-mitte, Jones, had
slumbering the lengtlGof the chain away.
In the movement of his arms as, lying on his side,
he wielded the short pick, Phillips could not
avoid jerking the links, and cofisequently Mr.
Jones’ repose had been disturbed, lie had remon-
strated with a lump of coal and an oath, and it
was under the influence of the pain excited by
the former striking him oil the face that Phillips,
in a hoarse .ivhisftet - , said: “God! that I could
bolt from this slavery !”
West was outsider in the adjacent gallery, his
mate, Hardy 7, like Jones, also preferring’to be
lazily 7 recumbent. West caught the whisper arid
echoed it: “I would to God I could !”
When lodging their—and their mates’—baskets
in the shaft chamber that night, Burt was spoken
to by Phillips. “ Would he make one to bolt!”
“Would I bolt from bell! Try me !”
# # #
Their unofficial masters, the Three with
the Records, discovered, it is impossible to say
how, the intention of their three slaves. Perhaps
the galleries carried the sound of the whispers;
perhaps in the disturbed sleep of one or other the
design was revealed. And learning it, the Three
adopted the notion. For a couple of days they
kept their subservient chain-mates on the inside,
while they themselves meeting at a point to which
their respective galleries converged, discussed the
possibilities.
M“ Them ’uns are for a-boltin’, are they?” said
Jones to Tooth, who was the discoverer of the
escaping scheme. “Now, we’ve got to go with
them or we’ve to die !” ,
“ That’s it sure!” responded Jones. “They
ain’t no chance of gettin’ away unless they kill
us, or we go with ’em. An’ we’ve been a-usin’
’em so that it’s not in reason they’ll want to take
us with ’em.”
Hardy took up the parable. “O’ cos not!
’ow cud they? But our lay’ll be to find out all
their plans, an’ then to blow ’em*, if they ain’t
good ’nuff to foller !”
; The three mouths which had known the
slavering were very close to one another,, and the
three heads to which they belonged touched. The
three bodies were crouching closely, so that no
syllables of the muttered infamies should pene-
trate to the men at the lower end of the chains.
“Hist!” said Jones. “An’ if they’re good
.’nuff, wot then? We ain’t a-goin’ to take them
along !”
“Yer a fool if yer don’t, then!” was
Hardy’s answer. “Wojb yer goin’ to live on till
yer reach the settled disrick? ”
The dreadful emphasis of his last phrase
invoked the reminiscent delight of his brother
gourmands. And in the next few moments' of
fieridish converse they developed their plans.
Which, after all, did not include the taking of the
Three Without with them. -
• # *
“Wot d’yer say to bolt, boys?” Jones took

up to the bottom of the shaft to bemusterid.
The Lieutenant-in-Command was exact. He
iriustered nightly. He would stand by the pit-
mouth, and looking-downwards could tell, in the

several heads. “Wot d’yer say ! ”
Phillips, Bunt and West sent at each other
accusatory glances. Who had told their oppressors?
Or had it been simply a coincidence that the other
three had framed a similar plan ? Usually sus-

fellows mumbled that “ he would make one to go.”-

“We’re goin’ to bolt, boys, an’.if as yer

The under-graduates’ assent wad not needed to

“ We’vts been a bit ’ard, but yer’d ha’

He paused. The graduates listened eagerly for

The five agreed. And then at Jon.es!.sugges-
or rather command, for none disputed his
assumption of the leadership, they took the Con-
vict Oath. They chanted the eight verses, which
began: '
t ’ • Hand to hand,
On Earth; in Hell,
-SickorWeH,
On Sea, on Land,
: On the Square, ever.
And ended—the intervening verses dare not be
quoted— ;
Stiff or in Breath,
Lag or Free, , . iit
.You and Me, ■ i
In Life, in Death,
. On the Cross, never.
They chanted them with crossed and re-crossed
hands, and the foot of each pressed to the foot of
another. And after the verses, the “ loving-cup ”
of blood. Not a very,copious draught—to provide
a hearty drink would have weakened" them, and
their leader was too much of the general to
occasion an unnecessary demand on their strength
—only a drop or two from each man’s open vein,
sucked by every other man. So was fealty to
their leader, honour to one another, plighted.
The Convict Oath was a terrible thing;,it was
never broken, without occasioning death to some-
one-not necessarily to the violator. John Price
died in 1857 because of a Convict Oath registered
at Norfolk Island in the Forties. •
•'* ' •
It was a terrible thing, that Oath. So the
undergraduates, at least, thought it, arid though
they had distrust and , hate of the graduates in
their hearts they were prepared to loyally, carry
out the compact. They.found it the easier to do
so because the murderers before him who had
gone through the horrid drill of “ starving for a
bolt ” helped them" in every way. \ >
The Three With not only did; not exact i from
. the Three Without any contribution of coal,' but
actually helped them. Jones filled two-thirds bf
Phillips’ basket one day in addition to his own ;
arid’Tooth ran the risk of a flogging on Inspection
Sunday because he kept Burit’s creel supplied!,
When half-way on the starving time, Bunt
: “ ja.cked-up ” on'half-rations 'and ' fell ill for a
couple of days. -V No, Bunty, my man, you
mustn’t take more meal—it’ll do you harm now.
. V . . s t , \ • V-V \ V
Stick to the half, and ye’ll be used to it in anothoi
day or two. Meantime, I’ll do your tally.”
Then the Three With put up the others t 6 all the
dodges as to economising food that they had .
learnt in the dread school of the Harbour. The
graduates completed the knowledge of the under-
graduates by showing how the proper sucking of a
bone was as good as meat, and how, if an ounce
of meat were chewed to a ball, then withdrawn -
from the mouth, and hardened bv, exposure to tire
a;r .it Would appease hunger as effectually as r
the mastication of the whole ration. They taught .
the neophytes how to extract the nutriment from
so'ap, .and the 1 best way of utilising, the candles.
“The. wick’s a meal in itself, Bunty—eat the
grease separately,” tenderly advised Tooth. -
But the main sustenance ,of the six 'during the
starving time was derived from the, subtle elixir
of hope. -
• # * *
The hour for the attempt was fixed for the
Saturday night midway between two Inspection
Sundays. , .
The Three With wanted it earlier —as early as
possible aftertlie next Inspection day. But the
Three Without, remembering Macquarie Harbour,
said to themselves that they would not then have
food enough. So it was fixed for the second
Saturday.
The grating was thrown open at five o’clock on
week-day mornings, but on Sundays an hour and ■
a half later.' That hour and a half would mean a "
longer start— and a good start was everything in a
“bolt.”
The first Inspection Sunday after the decision
was come to was passed safely. No one “split ”
or. “peached;” all tallies were -right except
Tooth’s. ’ On his score for a month he was five-
hundredweight short. Buthe took his “dozen”
as'mildly as the mother'takes the tiny blows of
her, infant’s pulpy fingers. And, when all was
said and done, what was twenty-five to Tooth,
who left Hell’s Gates with two thousand charged
against him on the books and impres.ed on his
back ?
That Sunday night they began the actual busi-
ness—the starving was merely a preliminary—of
escape. They fractured their irons—anklets,
waist-chains and-basils. Only the last were sawn
through hhtlT the 1 watch-nick. When the last
girdle was parted'they examined the saw eagerly.
-It was sjarcelyiSMiited ! ‘ I.‘. '
“To think of a-gettin’ off the. thing so :
ne’at !” cried Jones, affected to tears of gratitude.
“-Lor," ’tis much nicer than the ; col’ chisel!”
In tlje rare cases—one, say, in a hundred—that
a girdle’or basil once on was taken off, the cold
chisel was the cutting instrument. And cold
chisels had a nasty habit of slipping and gashing
the abdomen,' • • ;
# * *
With' tlfe release of their bodies from the
iron bands it became possible to achieve the next
step towards-consumniating the “bolt.”
The walls of the- shaft "were, - as has been indi
cated, timber-lined. -The planks were laid hori-
zontally, and the natural tendency of the Regime
to economy would t haye prompted the authorities
toleave .apertures between each plank, thus
making two do the work of three, had it not been
fob the consideration that a foot-hold would be
.gi.ven'td any convict venturing upon the escalade.
accordingly laid flush, without —
'so eohcluVied sifter/due examination—
affording a solitary Crevice _otr a' knot, which the
'ingenuity,of the prisoners could turn to'tile'least
account in mounting. 'Nevertheless, the six were
determined upon ascending. /;. *
The ’chamber' "at * the 1 bottom of the' pit, into
which the vein-galleries, and the.working and air-
drives opened, was ten feet deep.' That was the
first difficulty to surmount. 1 ...
Three irieh, bUn'Clied together, and "with.-,
round each other’s hecks and’bent heads,-stood
upright. ' ‘ . > h'
a On tKeir shoulders mounted two. And. upon
the shoulders of the two, the sixth riian—'Jones-
crept up, and wres’ted from its perpendiculars two
or three feet/above the'swaying'pillar'of bCStial

T he' next night three more T planks, were, hauled
down.’ The ’same' building!'up,'of the tottering,
sweating, pedestal of flesh—the same wrestling
with nails and cleats—the same great gaspirigs of
pain and relief as the planks : were pressed upon,
bent and gave way.
The third night, and the fourth night, and the
fifth night planks were loosened, taken out, and
then replaced loosely. Jones was getting near the
pit-mouth now, and was well within the rim of
light from the surface. He dare not leave the
gaps, for fear they might be observed from above.
The Men of the Regime were wont to discourage
ingenuity.
From the topmost of the loosened planks, it
would be easy to reach the walls at right angles
with the side on which the gaps had beep made.
Then, the last plank so extracted "would be used
to bridge the angle, and to form a standing-3tuge
for' the man who attacked the grating with the
watch-nick. And Jones was resolved that that
man should be himself. The man who filed away
the grating-bars would be first out of the shaft.
All this time the saved rations and candles were
accumulating. Heavens ! the daily horrors of
endurance and the frightful pangs suffered by
these men! The conquest over the griping hunger,
which might be gratified in a moment if they
would—the forcing of the energies to the Titanic
task of turning-out the allotted quantity per day
with semi-paralysed limbs and flaccid nerves—
the patience, the fortitude, the suffering—wduld
have been heroic in a' better cause—nay, were
heroic in'this very instance. “’ Was not this cause
' the sacred one of Freedom?

for* freedom !” cried’Phillips to Jones on the
seventh^night. • i

* # #
r i That was on the seventh night—a Saturday.
The next stage towards freedom was the drawing
of the dead- men’s rations.
Every morning rations were issued for the men
mustered overnight. The collective portion for
“ Murderers’ ” was sent down in the first baskets
in the morning.
The remaining Three had their own half rations
for the starvation, time on hand. Also, the like
quanity which had been accumulated by the self-
denial of the dead men. Still,- they had not
enough. They wanted more ! None of them
cared for setting out in company with his brother
gourmands with only a poor larder.
*- * #
On each of the eighth to the fifteenth nights
inclusive, the dead men were mustered. And on
each successive morning there came down in the
first basket the dead men’s rations.
Night after night would each of the still im-
prisoned Three place one of his liberated mates—
the one bp bad himself freed—against the back-
wall of the chamber and fix a candle in his haul.
And night after night would Lieutenant Cocke
look down—Audibly count, by the dips’flame, six
heads and six hands holding candles—and, having
“ mustered ” them, wish them all “ good niglP ! ’’
Night after night, till the fifteenth, were
the six mustered, and none of them made com-
plaint, and all drew their rations regularly the
next day. The dead as well as the quick drew
their rations. And the nostrils of the living..werq
filled with the odour of the dead, and they, too,
would have died, but that they were stayed by
the hope of liberty.
And on the fifteenth night, the other Three also
won Freedom, in a way to be told.
PRICR WARUNG.
The Socialists’ Position.
Dear Bulletin, Henry George, in “ Progress and
Poverty, shovys that refit limits wages, l and thence
assumes that, if land . were made common property, or
what is the same thing, if rent were confiscated by the
State, wages would increase to the fullearningsof labiur.
It is indeed true that so long as land is m mopolised
common wages will-never much exceed what a man car!
produce for himself from the poorest land in use, that is
to say, from the ,best land that can be had for the using,
and Will always tend to the minimum upon which
labourers can maintain their numbers; but it does not
follow that if land were nationalised wages would per-
manently rise, unless it were also shown that nothing else
existed to limit wages.
Where'George falls into error is in taking too limited a
view . of monopoly. Land ownership, railway; telegraph,
gas, and water companies, are not the onlv kinds of
monopoly. Every considerable aggregation of capital i 3
a monopoly, insomuch as it prevents men with little or no
capital from 6ng J aging in competitive industry. To Henry
George’s three economic laws a fourth must be added—the
law of capitalistic expropriation, which, like the law of
rent, but in opposition to the law of interest, “depends
on the margin of production, rising as it falls and falling
as it rises.” Destroy the monopoly of land, and wages
would not permanently rise, for the monopoly of capital
would take its -place, and would" fill' the bill in every
particular.
This can be shown from George’s own words, for,
although an. opponent of Socialism, which he does not
seem to correctly apprehend, there are many passages in
his books which show how the capitalist can oppress the
labourer—in fact, it is almost impossible to carefully and
thoughtfully read “Progress and Poverty” without
becoming convinced of the necessity of Socialism. In
Book 11., chap, iv., occurs a statement bearing on this
subject : “Twenty men working together will, where
Nature is niggardly, produce more than twenty times the
wealth that one man can produce where Nature is most
bountiful.” This assertion is undoubtedly true, for in
addition to the increased power derived from co-opera-
tion, twenty men can use machinery which one man could
not use.
The accepted law of rent is: “ That the rent (or value)
of land is determined by the excess of its produce over
that which the same application of labour and capital can
secure from the least productive land in use.” To this
should be added-the law of monopoly That the profits
of capital are determined by the “excess of wealth pro-
duced per head "by an indefinite number of men working
in concert, and with the aid of machinery, over that
which one man can produce working by himself, or if
you please, with the aid of his family.”
At present prices, a man without capital can make
but a bare living from the land, even in Australia,
where land is cheap, and an inspection of the foregoing
laws will render it obvious that if rent were abolished
wages could never rise much above a bare living, and
would again fall to the minimum as competition lowered
prices.
Suppose rent confiscated, and speculative rent elimi-
nated, economic rent would at once be largely reduced by
the-great extent of land now held for speculative purposes
being thrown open. A considerable portion of the capital
now engaged in commerce and manufactures would be
diverted by the fall in land-values to agricultural pur-
suits. This would tend to increase the prices of manu-
factured and imported articles, and to lower the prices of
agricultural produce.
If it is so difficult for the poor farmer to make a living
now, this alteration in relative prices would render it inc
possible, and the impossibility of making a living from
the-land without capital, would have the same effect upon
wages as the complete monopolisation* of the land—it
would reduce them to the minimum.
The nature of capital is that nothing can compete with
it in industry except capital; the great capitalist can
undersell anddrive out of the market the'small capitalist,
and the man who has not cauital has no resource but to-
take employment with the mail who has. -The.landowner
monopolises natural opportunities, but the capitalist
monopolises the tools and instruments of labour, without
which no man can apply his labour profitably, let natural
opportunities be ever so free.
Henry George's remedy, taken by itself, would be an
utter failure; when we nationalise land, we must at the
same time nationalise capital and-industry, without going
through the useless preliminary of the “Single-tax.” "
Broula, N.S.W.

space at-’dispbsal, to give/even ah outline of the
many processes through win the leaf has to pass,
befopb it emerges fit for use On the .upper floois
are the stripping and mixing departments, where
fi. bevy of Phyllises,' with, deft fihgbrs, remove tin-
stein from the leaf, and where it undergoes a
MR. H. STODDARD
process of coagulation, as it were. Then, below
stairs, are the various machines and apartments
for drying, pressing, cutting, twisting, folding,
packing, branding, etc., all of which necessitate
the employment of numerous hands. Among the
favourite brands may be mentioned, “ Good
Company,” most popular of high-class weeds, and
the solace of many a weary miner or solitary stock-
man; “Paragon,” the poorman’s friend—but there
are other brands, all of which have their admirers.
The customers of the Company are daily on the.
increase, and on the books are to be found the
names of numerous Sydney vendors. Operations
are not, however, confined solely to the manufac-
ture of locally-grown and imported-tobacco-leaf.
A very large business-is done—in-the-way—of
imputing , cigars -and .cigarette- tobaecoesrof- the-
Company’s‘own particular brands, and amongst,
the former may be specially mentioned the
“Cronoas,” “Fairies,” and “Matadors,” cigars,
which are manufactured specially to the Com-’
pany’s order. Then there are the celebrated
“ M.l>.” pipes, made for the Company, by one of;
the. veryA)4st houses in Vienna,- like other re-,
quisites for the borhfort of smokers. Two
which may be said to have considerably influenced*
the successful-operations-of- the Company have
been • without-doubt->the erection; of the very
ne.west machinery, and the employment of only
skilled labour. The chief-hands have been selected
on account of special ability, in -certain direc->
tions, and - , the managing foreman is an expert,
under whose ‘ charge' everything is done'in a‘
thoroughly systematic . manner.. The smart
business-manager,' Mr. Stoddart, is a great politi-
cian, "and is'at-the' present time ’so’ much
occupied'electioneering that he can scarcely afford!
the time to show visitors over the works. In his
department, however, he is the proper plug for the
round hole, and without a doubt much of the
concern’s success is; due to his skilful engineering,
remarkable energy and business push. The Com-
pany is one which deserves well .of the) tobacco-
smoking public, not only in the immediate
vicinity of Newcastle, but throughout the colony,
for its efforts to produce a ass article at the
lowest possible price, and* we venture to predict
that when Protection makes it profitable to enter
into competition with some of the lighter Ameri-
can brands, the Richmond Tobacfco Co. will step
right to the front.
The last surprise-party held in Melbourne
was a bad failure." ATot of lfind'friends called in
after the usual fashion at a mansion in a northern
suburb and deposited tjie ham on the keys of the
.pianoforte ; 'ancLdJjeir • bright smile haunts the
’proprietor* still? He came downstairs-gnashing
his teeth and foaming at the mouth,'and told the
.visitors that- he wished they had gout in both
feet—he meant to* say, his happiness was now com-
plete, and that he was pleased to self them all in*
—-that wits—ahem—he was pleased to see them all
well. Then he shook hands with them all round,
and said that he hoped they would all get into
gaol for life —here lie cleared lps throat hurriedly
—lie meant that lie would call down his wife.
Then the rest of the family -came down, and
greeted the visitors as effusively as if they were a
long-lost serpent or a bailiff; and they all looked
glad—as .glad as if their parents had been run
over by an omnibus. And the guests cleared
away the* furniture of the drawing-room, and
dropped greasy provisions all over the place, and
> roke some/bricja-braC'in the wildness of their
revelry, and danc d on the wild fantastic ex-
tremity till they shook a big oil-painting off the
wall and it went to impalpable smash on the floor,
when the host and hostess smiled like two hanged
murderers and said sweetly that it didn’t matter.
And the excitement grew wilder and wilder, and
the revels more frantic, and the noise more awful,
and in between the bursts of joy the hostess could
be heard breaking dishes in the back kitchen, and
calling on the Lord to strangle the whole
assemblage, and one bank-clerk winked eight
times at the eldest daughter till her father longed
to leap upon his gore. Finally they went away in
the small hours of the morning leaving chaos and
broken victuals everywhere behind them; and
then it was ascertained that all the jewellery had
been sneaked out of the bedrooms, and the head
of the house wanted to chase the departing
revellers with his gun, only the rest of the family
hung fainting on to his coat-tails and prevented
him. But next day he put the detectives on the
track of that surprise-party, and if he has his way
some of them will be badly surprised in Pentridge
at an early date.

Masonic Hall the other afternoon in the presence

While Colston, tljc Narbethong murderer,
was hiding in teetotal Premier Munro’s country-
house, he had only one square drink, and that
was when he swallowed the great cold-water
apostle's hair-wash. The wash consisted mostly
of rum, and the escaped maniac had a festivy
blow-out upon it before he hid himself up in the
attic. Considering that Munro has hardly a
solitary hair to-balance a hair-restorer upon the
circumstances look suspicious.
A lady ten feet round the waistns on view
just now in New York.. N.bW'jast suppose a man
won that lady’s affections and wanted to take her
in his arms, would it be advisable to squeeze lier
by putting a rope round her and hauling on the
two ends of it, or should he do it by walking
rapidly round her, and leaning up against her as
he went, or would it be better to shove her into a
corner and squeeze her against the wall? And
supposing a fellow proposed to her, and she.threw
herself into his arms, ought he to jump aside and
let her flop over on the floor, or should he rush at
her when she first begins to go off the perpendicu-
lar, and try to heave her back into an upright
position ? The Bulletin wants to know.

in The Bulletin,... -A— .
The Bulletin ip { an aggressive Dmhqeratio
paper, which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally.-Qondensed columns,‘all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
Started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
aryl lias become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a-vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavaijingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin's conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can atonce be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators,.inany
of which even took, so far as was ptsssiblp, its
name. They are now all dead, while wHe Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the-one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the" fact that for years it stood alone among
secular iiewspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress: it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
I The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
.that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed. r
i The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres'
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based ■
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s’
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact —
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,l
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from'
■one reader to another. -
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
■other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
■artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

To H. F. Kugelmakn, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest,
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorat.i ng every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
mad of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about, two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased ooming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
, P. KEENAN.
Declared anol subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty's
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1838.
Peter Smytiie, J.P.
